==PAGE:3==
Preface
This Master Thesis is a result of study performed at the Civil Engineering Department at the Technical Univer-
sity of Denmark. The project corresponds to 30 ECTS points.
The study was carried out from August 2018 to December 2018 at the International Centre for Indoor En-
vironment and Energy (ICIEE). It is a part of a research project called "Improved methodology for CO2 based
design of building ventilation", in a cooperation with Rambøll company. The study aims at the investigation
of the CO2 exposure level and the concentration dispersion in a common meeting room, and their importance
for ventilation design and indoor air quality assessment.
Kongens Lyngby, December 31, 2018
Tereza Snášelová (s150314)
==PAGE:4==
ii
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The design of ventilation systems and their performance assessments are often based on the indoor CO2 con-
centration. Standards and guidelines specify the categories of indoor environment and prescribe correspond-
ing limits for CO2 concentrations. The indoor CO2 concentration is commonly measured in the exhaust air,
assuming that the concentration in the exhaust and the inhaled air are the same, or at least close. Several design
methods and standard requirements are used and the resulting ventilation rates diﬀer in a wide range. There-
fore, it is diﬃcult to decide on the most appropriate way to deﬁne the ventilation rate. Ventilation rates must
be suﬃcient to provide healthy and comfortable indoor environment, however they should not be overlarge.
Unnecessarily high ventilation rates would lead to over-ventilation and to a substantial increase of energy use.
Demand control ventilation deals with these two issues. It is based on a real-time occupancy and a modula-
tion of ventilation rates according to the actual demand. Demand control ventilation can be based on the CO2
concentration measured by a CO2 sensor. However, current recommendations for the sensor placement are
vague, although an incorrect placement can rapidly worsen the performance of the ventilation system. Hence,
a better understanding of the relation between the CO2 exposure, the CO2 concentration in the exhaust air,
and the concentration distribution across the room is needed. The current assumptions in the standards and
guidelines should be veriﬁed and the current methods to deﬁne ventilation rates should be validated.
This study focuses on the realistic assessment of the CO2 exposure and the concentration dispersion under
various conditions. The importance of air distribution, heat load distribution, number and location of diﬀuser,
and the occupants’ behaviour were investigated during ten main full-scale experimental sessions. In simulated
full-scale meeting room, seven human subjects were the source of metabolic CO2. The CO2 exposure was
investigated via a breathing thermal manikin, simulating the eighth occupant. The CO2 concentration in the
inhaled air was measured in the artiﬁcial lungs connected to the manikin. CO2 gas was dosed in the exhaled
air of the manikin to simulate the metabolic CO2 generation by person and the CO2 concentration in the
inhaled air was measured in the artiﬁcial lungs connected to the manikin. This transient process with the
simulated exhalation of metabolic CO2 ensured the simulation of a human-like breathing process as close as
possible to reality: breathing frequency of the manikin was 10 min-1, pulmonary ventilation rate was 6 L/ min,
and the manikin was inhaling through mouth and exhaling through nose. The CO2 concentration was also
measured close to the breathing zone, in the occupied zone, at the walls, and in the air supply and the exhaust
terminal devices. Furthermore, the room air temperature, the mean air speed, the surface temperature, and
the supply and exhaust ﬂow rates were measured in order to control the ventilation system and to monitor the
environmental conditions in the room. Moreover, the correlation between the CO2 exposure and perceived air
quality was examined through subjective questionnaires.
The results showed substantial diﬀerences between the CO2 concentration in the air inhaled by the manikin
and the concentration in the exhaust air. The air distribution method deﬁnes the air ﬂow interaction in the
room and consequently aﬀects the CO2 exposure. Furthermore, parameters like heat load distribution, occu-
pants’ behaviour, or adequate design of the supply air characteristics have a considerable impact on the air ﬂow
interaction. The ceiling attached air supply diﬀusers located closer to the occupants’ breathing zone did not
show any substantial decrease in the inhaled CO2 concentration. The lowest inhaled CO2 concentration was
measured during the sessions with displacement air distribution. Interestingly, in the session with the highest
measured inhaled CO2 concentration, the perceived air quality by the subjects was rated as more acceptable
than in the session with the lowest inhaled CO2 concentration. Based on the results of this study, recommenda-
tions for ventilation rate design are proposed. The limits for the indoor CO2 concentration should be lowered
below the desired level of inhaled CO2 concentration. The adequacy of the calculation methods is dependent
==PAGE:6==
iv
Abstract
on appropriate assumptions. Although more research is needed to conﬁrm the proposed recommendations,
they can potentially decrease the CO2 exposure of the occupants and improve the indoor air quality.

CHAPTER1
Introduction
This chapter presents the background for understanding the importance of CO2 in ventilation design, and
therefore the signiﬁcance of better insights into its dispersion in a room. In the beginning, the term indoor air
quality (IAQ) is introduced, with focus on CO2 as an indoor pollutant and current indicator of the actual indoor
air quality. Next, an overview of standards on designing ventilation rates is presented. Various standards
prescribe several diﬀerent ways to deﬁne design criteria for ventilation rates, in terms of particular calculations
or tabular values. The methods are often based on CO2 concentrations. Subsequently, a summary of those
calculation methods is oﬀered. Finally, an introduction into demand control ventilation is presented with the
main focus on CO2 based control.
1.1
Indoor air quality
One of the main reasons for providing healthy and comfortable indoor environment is that people spend
an increasing time indoors. Indoor environment is a complex issue including thermal comfort, indoor air
quality, acoustics, and lighting. It is often diﬃcult to provide the best conditions in all aspects of the indoor
environment. Therefore, a compromise in the design process might be made. There are numerous guidelines
introducing indoor environment categories [1, 2] and recommending limits for each of the component of the
indoor environment.
The health aspect of IAQ is based on occupants’ exposure to various pollutants in the indoor air. Those
pollutants are either generated outdoor and penetrate into the indoor environment, or produced indoor, for
instance as a result of occupants’ activities, or emissions from products used indoor [3]. Some health eﬀects
can occur shortly after a single exposure to a pollutant, some can show up after long or repeated periods of
exposure [4]. Moreover, the reaction to a pollutant diﬀers substantially between people, depending on their
individual sensitivity. Some people can even become sensitized to a pollutant after repeated or high level
of exposures [4]. Another eﬀect related to the IAQ is the so-called sick building syndrome (SBS). The sick
building syndrome includes symptoms like eyes, nose and throat problems (including irritation), drowsiness,
headache, diﬃculties to concentrate, or fatigue [5, 6].
The amount of clean air, perceived air freshness and odors, relative humidity, and air temperature and
velocity are just some of the components aﬀecting the perceived air quality. Numerous studies focused on this
issue [7–11]. A signiﬁcant source of indoor pollution are the occupants themselves, as they are producing CO2
and other bioeﬄuents. The threshold limits of CO2 presented by the Association Advancing Occupational
and Environmental Health (ACGIH) are 5 000 ppm as the time weighted average threshold limit value, and
30 000 ppm as the short term exposure limit for 15 minutes [12]. These values are usually not obtained under
common conditions indoors. Nevertheless, while CO2 does not reach harmful levels, it is very often used as an
indicator of IAQ. There are some SBS symptoms and other outcomes related to elevated CO2 levels, but these
associations might be due to lower ventilation rates and therefore elevated concentrations of other pollutants,
rather than to CO2 itself [13]. In fact, there were numerous studies focusing on higher CO2 concentration and
its impact on the occupants’ well-being and performance. However, these studies oﬀer dissimilar conclusions.
Satish et al. (2012) studied the impact of CO2 concentration of 600, 1 000, and 2 500 ppm on decision making.
The study showed that increased indoor CO2 concentrations were associated with a statistically signiﬁcant
decrease of the decision-making performance [14]. The summarized results of the study are presented in
Figure 1.1. A negative impact on mental performance of subjects was reported in other studies as well [15]. On
==PAGE:20==
2
1 Introduction
the contrary, no changes in performance were reported under increasing CO2 concentration up to 3 000 ppm
by Liu et al. [16].
Figure 1.1: Impact of CO2 on human decision-making performance presented by Satish et al. [14]
1.1.1
Assessment of IAQ
As mentioned above, indoor CO2 is not of big interest as a health-harmful pollutant. Nevertheless, it has
been used to characterize IAQ conditions in buildings and to estimate building ventilation rates. Perceived air
quality may be assessed based on percentage of dissatisﬁed, i.e. un-adapted persons who perceive the air as
unacceptable just after entering a space. ASHRAE Standard 62 considers acceptable air quality as a condition,
when the air quality is accepted by minimum of 80 % of the occupants. In another words, the acceptable
air quality corresponds to 20 % of dissatisﬁed [17]. European standards use diﬀerent levels of percentage of
dissatisﬁed to classify diﬀerent indoor environment categories.
The concept of indoor environment categories or classes helps to deﬁne proper design criteria for indoor
environment during the design process. The standards [1, 2, 18] fully utilize the categories for recommending
design operative temperature, mean velocities in the occupied zone, acoustic levels, etc. There are buildings
and spaces that require higher standards and better performance due to their usage, like premises for very
young children or for elderly people. It is expected that utilizing a high quality indoor environment requires
higher initial and operational costs. Buildings for a common use are usually designed to meet less strict
criteria. All occupied buildings must meet the requirements for healthy indoor environment. Therefore, to
provide clear and comprehensive recommendations for design criteria, indoor environment is classiﬁed in
categories. The indoor environment categories are listed in Table 1.1. These categories can be found in some
other standards as well [18, 19], but named diﬀerently.
Correspondingly, these categories also specify IAQ requirements. According to [1, 2] the categories corre-
spond to the percentage of dissatisﬁed of 15 %, 20 %, and 30 % for category A, B, and C, respectively. Moreover,
there is a deﬁned relation between the percentage of dissatisﬁed (PD) and the CO2 concentration above out-
door level. The relation is given by
P D = 395 × e(−15,15×C−0,25
CO2 ),
(1.1)
where CCO2 is the CO2 concentration above outdoor level (ppm). Additionally, minimum ventilation rates
have been proposed based on the percentage of dissatisﬁed, as shown in Figure 1.2 [2].

only one maximum allowed value for indoor CO2, some oﬀer diﬀerent limits for the indoor environment
categories. Moreover, the limits can be presented as an absolute value, or as an indoor CO2 concentration
above outdoor level. Table 1.2 presents CO2 concentration limits recommended in some of the standards and
guidelines. Those values are also used as a basis for IAQ evaluation in existing buildings.

==PAGE:22==
4
1 Introduction
In some existing buildings, it is required to evaluate the performance of the building systems in terms of
providing desired indoor environment conditions. The needed measurements are described in DS/EN 15251,
including speciﬁcations on how to conduct the measurements, and recommended criteria for acceptable devi-
ations. Usually, the inspection regarding IAQ includes basic ventilation rate measurements. Only if speciﬁc
complaints about odor or SBS symptoms remain despite provided suﬃcient ventilation rates, measurements
of speciﬁc pollutants should be made. However, in buildings where occupants are the main pollution source,
CO2 measurements can be used for a ventilation rate evaluation. The standard states that the measurement
should be made where occupants spend most of their time, preferably at head level during typical high load
conditions [1]. Those measurements are preferred to be performed in winter season, when the supply ﬂow rate
is usually lower. Additionally, it is possible to conduct the measurement during times with the worst condi-
tions, e.g. in the morning or in the end of the afternoon for oﬃce buildings or schools. Also, it is not necessary
to perform the measurements in all rooms in the building. Around 5 - 10 % of representatively chosen rooms
might be suﬃcient for this purpose. The requirements for measuring instruments for evaluation of the supply
air ﬂow rate are speciﬁed in DS/EN 12599 [22].
1.2
Ventilation rate design
The purpose of ventilation in buildings is to provide occupants with clean air for breathing and to dilute indoor
air pollution. Additionally, supply air can also help with providing thermally comfortable environment. The
design of a ventilation or air-conditioning system depends among others on the amount of supply air (e.g. size
and power of the HVAC unit, dimensions of ventilation ducts, etc.). However, there are diﬀerent methods and
approaches for how to determine the ventilation rate, based on various parameters of a speciﬁc case. Those
parameters include the type of building, the pollution emissions from building component, the occupant
density, the occurrence of contaminants indoor, etc. The methods aim at supplying suﬃcient ventilation rates
regarding either health or acceptable perceived air quality.
Together with the proposed approaches and design values, the designer’s judgment and assumptions are
an essential part of the design process. Many of the standards prescribe only minimum requirements and
encourage designers to adjust the value to every single case. Another key point mentioned in the standards is
the eﬀort to minimize the pollution emission caused by the ventilation or air-conditioning system itself. This
implies a use of suitable components and also proper operation and maintenance of the system.
This section introduces an overview of four standards regarding indoor environment and ventilation de-
sign criteria. The review focuses on recommendations and calculations methods for ventilation rates with
diﬀerent approaches and prerequisites. Some of the standards further contain recommendations and design
criteria for indoor environment as whole, i.e. including thermal comfort, lighting, and acoustics [1, 2]. Some
of the standards present guidelines for design and operation of ventilation or air-conditioning systems [17, 18].
However, these matters are not a subject of this review.
CEN CR 1752
The CEN report "Ventilation for Buildings - Design criteria for the indoor environment" speciﬁes the re-
quirements for the indoor environment and among others presents methods for the design of ventilation and
air-conditioning systems [2]. It focuses on the indoor environment where the human occupation is the main
concern, but it does not cover dwellings. Similarly to EN standards (European Norms), it divides indoor envi-
ronment into three categories, speciﬁed in the CEN report as categories A, B, and C.
1This publication is partly based on the Danish indoor climate classiﬁcation standard (DS 3033 Voluntary classiﬁcation of the quality
of the indoor climate in residential houses, schools, childrens’ day-care centres and oﬃces) and partly on the Finnish indoor environment
classiﬁcation scheme. [21]
2CO2 level valid for day-care facilities, teaching rooms at schools, etc. [20]
3CO2 level recommended for oﬃce buildings, schools and kindergartens.
4CO2 level recommended for residential buildings.
==PAGE:23==
1.2 Ventilation rate design
5
All the proposed design values or calculations of ventilation rates include several primary parameters that
must be considered and that can aﬀect the choice of the design criteria or methods. Those parameters are the
ventilation eﬀectiveness, the quality of the outdoor air, the level of building pollution, or type of pollution
sources indoor. For instance, it is up to a designer to assume whether the occupants are the only pollution
source in a space or not. In addition, the CEN report includes recommendations for the design of spaces
occupied by smokers.
Generally speaking, it is always important to verify whether the prescribed design criteria in the standard
apply for the parameters of the speciﬁc case. The primary ventilation rates are given in Table 1 in the report [2],
where they are divided according to the type of building and the indoor environment category. Those values
are based on the assumptions that the ventilation eﬀectiveness equals to 1 (i.e. the supplied outdoor air is
completely mixed with the polluted room air), the outdoor air is of excellent quality and the building is a
low-polluting one. Design values for not low-polluting buildings are given in annex C of CEN CR 1752. The
design values for the ventilation rate if the occupants are the only source of pollution are given as well, as
presented in Table 1.3.
Table 1.3: Required ventilation rate per occupant in spaces where the occupants are the only source of pollu-
tion, according to CEN CR 1752 [2]
Required ventilation rate (L/s × occupant)
Category
No smoking
20 % smokers
40 % smokers5
100 % smokers6

12
Furthermore, CEN CR 1752 prescribes how the required ventilation rates should be determined with re-
spect to the health risks and to the comfort. The ventilation required for controlling the health risk from
a speciﬁc pollutant and the ventilation required to obtain and maintain the requested perceived air quality
should be deﬁned and calculated separately. The higher value from those two should be used for the further
design. Ordinarily, the comfort criterion usually requires higher ventilation rates.
The ventilation required for health is determined based on the pollution load of a chemical in the space, as
well as on the outdoor concentration of this chemical in the air intake. It is therefore necessary to identify the
critical chemicals in the space and their pollution load. The ventilation rate for health is given by
qh =
Gh
Ch,i −Ch,o
× 1
εv
,
(1.2)
where qh is the ventilation rate required for health (L/s), Gh is the pollution load of a chemical (µg/s), Ch,i
is the guideline value of a chemical (µg/L), Ch,o is the outdoor concentration of a chemical at air intake (µg/L),
and εv is the ventilation eﬀectiveness [2]. For cases where the ventilation eﬀectiveness is unknown, the report
advises to assume εv being equal to 1. Equation 1.2 can be also used to deﬁne the ventilation rate for comfort
based on CO2 as an indicator, if the occupants are assumed to be the only source of pollution. CEN CR 1752
also states the CO2 generation rate per occupant and for various physical activity levels. The generation rate
of an occupant with the sedentary activity level according to the report is 19 L/h.
The comfort aspect is connected to the perceived air quality, and thus to the sensory pollution. The sensory
pollution sources are usually the occupants and the building, namely building materials, furniture, equipment,
5For 40-100 %, the required ventilation rate is equal to the value of 40 % smokers, since smokers are more tolerant towards environ-
mental tobacco smoke than non-smokers [2].
6See footnote 5.
==PAGE:24==
6
1 Introduction
or even ventilation systems. The pollution loads are given in olfs, which can be understood in the sensory
pollution terms as a person equivalent. One olf is a pollution generated by an average sedentary adult oﬃce
worker feeling thermally neutral. Another unit used in the ﬁeld of the perceived air quality is decipol (dp).
One decipol is deﬁned as the air quality in a space with a pollution source strength of one olf, ventilated
by 10 L/s of clean air [2]. Consequently, one decipol equals to 0,1 olf/(L/s). The report gives design values
of sensory pollution for occupant with diﬀerent activity level and for some types of buildings, as well as
corresponding perceived air quality in decipols for the three indoor environment categories. The required
ventilation rate for comfort is then given by
qc = 10 ×
Gc
Cc,i −Cc,o
× 1
εv
,
(1.3)
where qc is the ventilation rate required for comfort (L/s), Gc is the sensory pollution load (olf), Cc,i is the
desired perceived indoor air quality (dp), Cc,o is the perceived outdoor air quality at air intake (dp), and εv is
the ventilation eﬀectiveness [2]. Both equations 1.2 and 1.3 are based on steady-state conditions.
The perceived air quality corresponding to the building categories is given either in decipols or by the
percentage of dissatisﬁed visitors. The relation between the percentage of dissatisﬁed (PD) and the ventilation
rate is presented in Figure 1.2.
DS/EN 15251
The European Norm EN 15251, accepted as Danish standard DS/EN 15251, speciﬁes the indoor environ-
mental parameters and how to apply them for the system design and the energy performance calculations.
Moreover, it also includes methods for monitoring and evaluating the indoor environment in existing build-
ings, as mentioned in section 1.1.1. The standard is applicable for non-industrial buildings with the indoor en-
vironment criteria based on the human occupancy. Unlike CEN CR 1752, this standard covers also dwellings.
DS/EN 15251 proposes several methods and diﬀerent design values for ventilation rates in non-residential
buildings. The ventilation rates depend on the usual parameters, like occupancy level, activity of the oc-
cupants, processes, and emissions from the building components. Annex B of the standard presents three
methods of calculating the required ventilation rates [1].
The ﬁrst method takes into account both the occupants and the building components. The total ventilation
rate is deﬁned as
qtot = nocc × qP + A × qB,
(1.4)
where qtot is the total ventilation rate of the room (L/s), nocc is the number of occupants, qP is the ven-
tilation rate for occupancy (L/s per person), A is the room ﬂoor area (m2), and qB is the ventilation rate for
emission from building (L/s per m2) [1]. The values for qP and qB are given for the three categories and also
for a diﬀerent level of building pollution. However, it should be noted that the prescribed values are based on
complete mixing and must be adjusted according to the speciﬁc ventilation eﬀectiveness. As it is mentioned in
DS/EN 13779, the ventilation eﬀectiveness can vary even for basically correct conﬁgurations, since it depends
on a signiﬁcant number of parameters [18]. Therefore, this is a considerable disadvantage of the method. The
annex B of DS/EN 15251 oﬀers examples of recommended ventilation rates for occupancy based on a typical
occupancy density given in L/s per m2.
Another approach presented in DS/EN 15251 is based on a deﬁned ventilation rate per person or per
m2 ﬂoor area. Speciﬁcally, it requires the assumption that the occupants are the only source of pollution,
or it assumes only the pollution from material emissions. The recommended ventilation rates according to
==PAGE:25==
1.2 Ventilation rate design
7
DS/EN 15251 are shown in Table 1.4. Since the recommendations are not strictly required, it may be preferable
in practice to adjust the method to a speciﬁc case by adding ventilation rate for building emission pollution or
deﬁne the ventilation rate by diﬀerent ways and choose the highest one.
Table 1.4: Recommended ventilation rates for non-residential buildings per person, and per ﬂoor area for
three types of building components’ pollution. Rates are given per person or per m2 ﬂoor area, according to
DS/EN 15251[1]

Finally, DS/EN 15251 also suggest calculation of the ventilation rate based on CO2 concentration in the
room. The calculation is based on a mass balance for the CO2 concentration and refers to DS/EN 13799 for
further description (the DS/EN 13779 is discussed in the following). Equally to the ﬁrst presented method,
these methods also assume complete mixing and the ventilation eﬀectiveness equal to 1.
For the non-residential buildings, DS/EN 15251 gives recommendations for ventilation during un-occupied
hours. One way is based on zero ventilation during un-occupied hours, with a short period of ventilation be-
fore the occupancy. It recommends that two air volumes of the ventilated space should be delivered into the
space before the start of the occupancy. The other way assumes a continuous minimum ventilation during the
un-occupied periods. This minimum ventilation rate should be based on the building type and the pollution
load. However, it should be 0,1 - 0,2 L/s per m2, if national requirements do not specify diﬀerently.
Similarly to non-residential buildings, the ventilation rates for residential buildings depend on many pa-
rameters such as the number of occupants, the emission from resident activities and furnishing, etc. Humidity
in dwellings is of great importance, as it is related to several adverse health eﬀects, such as respiratory infec-
tions, asthma, or allergic rhinitis [23]. At the same time, there are certain sources of humidity in the dwellings,
for instance cooking or bathing. The ventilation rates are either speciﬁed as an air change rate per hour, as a
supply or exhaust rate, or as an overall air change rate of the entire building [1]. Examples of ventilation rates
for dwellings are presented in annex B in DS/EN 15251.
DS/EN 13779
The full title of this European Standard is "Ventilation for non-residential buildings - Performance require-
ments for ventilation and room-conditioning systems". As the title implies, this standard focuses on the system
aspects, design, and system performance. Therefore, it includes among others recommendations regarding air
ﬁlters, speciﬁc fan power, and reuse of extract air. It also deﬁnes minimum distances between air intake and
exhaust openings. It further provides a brief recommendation on the ventilation rates under various room
parameters.
The standard classiﬁes diﬀerent types of air appearing in ventilation and air-conditioning systems, for
instance outdoor air, supply air, transferred air, recirculation and secondary air. Moreover, it describes the
classiﬁcation of chosen types of air. The indoor air is sub-divided into four categories, namely IDA 1, 2, 3,
and 4. Those categories refer to high, medium, moderate, and low indoor air quality, respectively. While
DS/EN 13779 does not provide a complete deﬁnition of indoor air quality categories, it refers to DS/EN 15251
as a possible reference. Additionally, it mentions that the design criteria should be satisﬁed in the occupied
zone and gives typical dimensions for the occupied zone. While the requirements can be measured anywhere
in the total area of the room, the comfort criteria are not guaranteed beyond the occupied zone [18].
==PAGE:26==
8
1 Introduction
In comparison with DS/EN 15251, DS/EN 13779 gives an advice regarding the ventilation rate for short
emission-periods. In such cases, the steady-state might not be achieved, or the air ﬂow can be reduced for a
given maximum concentration level [18].
Annex A of this standard includes among others brief recommendation of ventilation rates. The important
parameter for these design values is whether the room is designed for human occupancy or not. Speciﬁcally,
DS/EN 13779 prescribes rates of outdoor or transferred air per unit ﬂoor area for rooms not designed for
human occupancy, or ventilation rates of outdoor air per person. The standard mentions that if applicable,
the designed outdoor air rate based on occupancy can also take into account emissions from the building.
The recommended ventilation rates per person according to DS/EN 13779 are presented in Table 1.5. The
standard also prescribes ventilation rates of outdoor air for rooms not designed for human occupancy, listed
in the standard in Table A.9 [18]. It must be remembered that those values are based on 50 % of running time
and spaces with the room height up to 3 m. If the running time is shorter or the room height is bigger the
ventilation rates should be increased accordingly.

ANSI/ASHRAE Standard 62
The purpose of the American standard for ventilation for acceptable indoor air quality, ASHRAE Standard
62 [17], is to specify minimum ventilation rates and other measures intended to provide indoor air quality
acceptable to human occupants. It deﬁnes requirements for design of ventilation and air-conditioning systems,
their installation, commissioning, and maintenance. On the contrary to CEN CR 1752 and DS EN 15251, the
ASHRAE Standard 62 does not include any considerations of control of thermal comfort and does not contain
any recommendations for ventilation of spaces containing smoking. Similarly to DS/EN 13779, this standard
also focuses on the ventilation system design. Among others, it speciﬁes the distance between outdoor air
intakes and potential outdoor contaminant source. It recommends an investigation of the outdoor air quality
prior to completing ventilation system design. Such an observational survey should be done in the immediate
surrounding of a building site during hours when the building is expected to be normally occupied. The
==PAGE:27==
1.2 Ventilation rate design
9
survey should identify prospective local contaminants. The observation shall focus on odors or irritants, and
visible plumes or visible air contaminants, as well as description of sources of vehicle exhaust on site and on
adjoining properties [17].
The standard introduces three procedures for ventilation design, namely the ventilation rate procedure,
the indoor air quality (IAQ) procedure, and the natural ventilation procedure. In addition, together with the
determined outdoor supply air ﬂows, requirements for the exhaust ventilation must be followed for the given
types of spaces. Those are for instance printing rooms, barber shops, commercial kitchens, or locker rooms.
The ventilation rate procedure determines the outdoor air ﬂow rates based on the occupancy level, the
type of building, and the ﬂoor area. The approach is similar to the previously reviewed standards, however
the required air ﬂows per person or per m2 ﬂoor area prescribed in Standard 62 are diﬀerent. The supply air
ﬂow required in the breathing zone of the occupied space is deﬁned as the breathing zone outdoor air ﬂow.
The breathing zone outdoor air ﬂow should not be less than the value given by
Vbz = Rp × Pz + Ra × Az,
(1.5)
where Vbz is the breathing zone outdoor air ﬂow (L/s), Rp is the outdoor air ﬂow rate required per person
(L/s per person), Pz is the number of people in the ventilation zone during typical usage, Ra is the outdoor air
ﬂow rate required per unit area (L/s per m2), and Az is the zone ﬂoor area (m2) [17]. It is important to mention,
that the values for outdoor air ﬂow rate per person given in the standard are based on adapted occupants and
all other applicable requirements of the standard being met. This is in contrast with CEN CR 1752, where
the percentage of dissatisﬁed is based on visitors, i.e. not-adapted people entering the room. Similarly to
the other standards, the ventilation rate can be deﬁned on the exact parameters or by using default values.
However, the standard does not distinguish between diﬀerent indoor environment classes, but it provides
only one minimum required rate for various types of spaces. Table 1.7 presents minimum ventilation rates in
the breathing zone for some types of spaces, the full table contains 81 diﬀerent types of spaces [17].
Table 1.7: Minimum ventilation rates in breathing zone, according to ASHRAE Standard 62 [17]

rameter substituting the ventilation eﬀectiveness is introduced as the zone air distribution eﬀectiveness Ez and
its default values are prescribed in the standard. Examples of Ez are presented in Table 1.8. Consequently, the
supply air ﬂow rate provided to the ventilation zone is given by
Voz = Vbz/Ez,
(1.6)

Likewise CEN CR 1752 [2], this standard presents design criteria for varying operating conditions. Under
certain conditions the design can be done using average conditions over a time period. This is applicable for
two cases: it is known that the peak occupancy will be of short duration, or that the ventilation will vary for a
short period of time.
The IAQ procedure deﬁnes the system design parameters including the building outdoor air intake rate,
based on the analysis of contaminant sources and limits, and the level of perceived indoor air acceptability [17].
This procedure allows to meet higher performance goals for IAQ than with the minimum requirements pre-
scribed in the ventilation rate procedure [24]. According to the IAQ procedure, the designer should identify
the main contaminants or mixtures of concern, as well as determine the emission rates for each contaminant.
Regarding the contaminants’ concentration limits, the standard only presents an informative appendix with
concentration guidelines for some prospective contaminants of concern. Consequently, the zone supply air
ﬂow rate should be the larger of the mass balance analysis and the subjective evaluation, further speciﬁed
in the standard. In appendix D of ASHRAE Standard 62, acceptable mass balance equations for the use of
the IAQ procedure are presented. There are six equations in total, depending on the ﬁlter location and the
required recirculation rate. Moreover, the mass balance equations include among others the recirculation ﬂow
factor, the ﬁlter eﬃciency, and the zone air distribution eﬀectiveness [17].

1.2.1
Comparison of ventilation rate calculations
This section compares the methods for determining ventilation rates in the design process. It is obvious that
each standard has its own prescribed room prerequisites and the recommended values for ventilation rates
are based on slightly diﬀerent parameters. Therefore, in order to provide clear and comprehensive summary
of the mentioned methods, a comparison of the ventilation rate calculations is presented. Table 1.9 and 1.10
include the speciﬁcation of the standard, the calculation including default values given by the standard, and
assumptions for the calculated ventilation rate. The ventilation rates are calculated based on concrete values
implying from the assumptions below, or on values given by tables in the standards, i.e. tabular values. All
the presented methods are based on steady-state conditions. Furthermore, the calculations are based on the
following assumptions7:
• the space/room type is a meeting room or a conference room
• the calculated ventilation rate is for one room/zone
• the ﬂoor area equals to 17,3 m2 and the volume of the room is 50 m3
• there are 8 occupants in the room
• the room comes under the indoor environment category B, if any category is required
• the ventilation eﬀectiveness is equal to 1,0
• the room is assumed as low-polluting
• no smokers are assumed in the room
7The assumptions are based on the same conditions as were in the actual experiment presented further in this study. Therefore, it
is possible to compare the calculated supply air ﬂow rates according to the diﬀerent standards with the supply air ﬂow rate used in the
study.

As can be seen from Table 1.9 and 1.10, the resulting ventilation rates vary substantially. The lowest ven-
tilation rate is 24,8 L/s (by ASHRAE Standard 62), the highest ventilation rate is 114,4 L/s (by DS/EN 13779).
These diﬀerences have considerable impacts on design of the ventilation system, the initial and operating costs,
the energy use, and the resulting PAQ. Smaller ventilation rates enable design of a ventilation system with
smaller dimensions. On the contrary, the achieved PAQ might not be acceptable for the occupants. This is the
case of ASHRAE standard, although it clearly states that the prescribed values are based on adapted occupants.
DS/EN 13779 proposes the highest ventilation rates that would clearly satisfy the occupants’ PAQ, but at the
same time lead to substantially bigger dimensions of the ventilation system. CEN CR 1752 and DS/EN 15251
oﬀer ten calculation methods in total. The resulting ventilation rates are in the range of 56,0 L/s to 92,3 L/s. To
illustrate the impact of diﬀerent ventilation rates, Table 1.11 presents the resulting CO2 concentration in the
exhaust from the room for all the listed calculation methods. The CO2 concentration is calculated based on

• the calculation is based on steady-state conditions
• the CO2 concentration in the outdoor air is 400 ppm
• there are 8 occupants in the room
• the generation rate of the occupants is 19 L/h [2]
• the ventilation eﬀectiveness is equal to 1,0

1.3 CO2 based control of ventilation systems
15
1.3
CO2 based control of ventilation systems
In buildings and spaces with irregular occupancy schedule it is convenient to design ventilation or air condi-
tioning systems based on demand controlled operation. This way helps to avoid unnecessary over-ventilation
of spaces that can occur if the ventilation rate is based on the assumed peak occupancy. Moreover, it allows to
optimize the operational costs and to decrease the use of energy. Demand Control Ventilation (DCV) is based
on a real-time occupancy and a modulation of ventilation rates according to an actual demand. It does not
aﬀect the design ventilation capacity required for the space, but it only controls the operation of the system to
be closer to the actual usage [25].
There are various technologies to count zone population, each of them more suitable for diﬀerent types of
space. Available zone population sensing techniques are time-of-day schedules, occupancy (on/oﬀ) sensors,
people counters, and CO2 sensors [26]. While time-of-day schedules are an adequate technique for spaces like
lecture halls (with known number of students enrolled for the course) or school cafeterias (with known time of
a day when most of the students arrive), CO2 based DCV is more suitable for spaces with unpredictable vari-
ations in occupancy. The CO2 based DCV is suitable for buildings and zones like cinemas, theaters, teaching
labs, meeting rooms, or retail establishments.
In order to obtain the desired function and consecutive savings, the DCV system must be designed and
installed properly. One of the factors aﬀecting the DCV operation are the CO2 sensors. Commonly used tech-
nologies are the non-dispersive infrared detection and the photo-acoustic infrared spectroscopy. The desired
characteristics of the sensors are high accuracy and minimal required maintenance, including the calibration
of the instrument.
Another key point of the design of DCV system is the location of the CO2 sensor. The correct placement
of the sensors signiﬁcantly aﬀects the functioning of the system and the success of the DCV concept. There
are no strict requirements on the sensor location and the recommendations given in standards or guidelines
are rather unspeciﬁc. In the current practice, two main options for the sensor location exist, namely in the
exhaust duct or wall-mounted. However, it is recommended to install the sensors in the occupied space rather
than in the ducting [25, 26]. The installation of duct sensors is possible in single-zone systems or in multiple-
zone systems with similar occupancy pattern in the zones. The most frequently used locations for the sensor
installation in the ducting are directly in the return air duct or inside the return air plenum.
The preferred placement for the CO2 sensor is at the wall, with similar criteria as for temperature sen-
sors. The sensor should be mounted at a mid-height in a room, but not very close to the occupants. People
breathing at the sensor would considerably aﬀect the readings, so the current practice recommends locations
at least 0,6 m from the prospective occupants [25]. Moreover, the sensor should be placed away from doorways,
radiators, windows, and air inlet devices.
==PAGE:34==
16
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER2
Justiﬁcation
The eﬀort to substantially decrease energy consumption implies the assessment of the energy use in buildings
and their technical services in order to increase their energy eﬃciency. According to the International Energy
Agency (IEA), buildings use 30 % of the global ﬁnal energy consumption, which is comparable to industry
(excluding constructions) or transport with an energy consumption equal to 31 % and 28 %, respectively [27].
A substantial part of this energy is used for HVAC systems that provide thermal comfort and good indoor
air quality to occupants. A proper design of the HVAC system is essential for both providing healthy and
comfortable indoor environment, and eﬃcient energy use. Therefore, an adequate ventilation design is of a
great importance.
As presented in the previous chapter, ventilation rate design is closely related to the CO2 concentration
indoor. The design of a ventilation system is often based on the diﬀerence between the outdoor and the
indoor CO2 level. The standards present various limits, depending on the building type or category. However,
none of the standards clearly speciﬁes where this concentration should be measured. They state that the
introduced limits are conditioned by a fully mixed environment with a ventilation eﬀectiveness equal to 1,0.
However, this condition is hardly achieved in practice [18]. Hence, the current common practice of measuring
CO2 concentration in the exhaust air might not correctly represent the actual state in the room and CO2
concentration level in the air inhaled by the occupants. Therefore, further research on the impact of various
parameters, such as type and distribution of heat and CO2 generation sources, supply airﬂow pattern, etc., on
the CO2 concentration distribution in the room is required.
In general, air distribution and subsequent ﬂow interaction in a room are rather complex issues aﬀected
by numerous factors. Initially, the ﬂow patterns in a ventilated room are a result of designed air distribution,
for instance mixing, displacement, or other method. The supply ﬂow rate, the location and the type of the
air inlet are important as well. Once the air enters the room it interacts with other air currents generated by
warmer or colder surfaces, buoyancy ﬂows generated by heat sources, or air ﬂow generated by local devices.
All of this has an eﬀect on the ﬂow interaction and together with the pollution (heat or contaminant) source lo-
cation, also on the dispersion of CO2 concentration in the room. Mahyuddin and Awbi [28] studied the spatial
distribution of CO2 under laboratory conditions. Their focus was on the CO2 dispersion under mixing venti-
lation with diﬀerent air change rate of clean or recirculated air. The air was supplied through one rectangular
slot (400 mm x 65 mm), located 2,18 m above the ﬂoor on a wall (just below the ceiling). The measurements
were performed in an environmental chamber, with two occupants performing common classroom work. The
used measuring equipment was a 12-channel gas analyzer, similar to the one described later in section 4.4.1.1,
with a relatively slow response time. The study showed that higher CO2 concentrations were located in higher
elevations. Most of the highest values were observed at 1,8 m and not at the height of 1,2 m. Even though the
high ventilation rate (8 L/s) provided relatively uniform CO2 concentration patterns, in practise a use of only
one CO2 sensor could lead to inaccurate estimations of the CO2 level [28]. Therefore, sensors located at two
or more heights were recommended. However, this study comprised only mixing ventilation achieved by one
type of air supply and did not investigate other air distribution methods. In fact, the study did not aim at
investigating the impact of the ventilation strategy on the exact exposure level.
The study of Mahyuddin and Awbi did not include observation of concentration levels at walls. On the
contrary, Fisk et al. [29] conducted multi-point measurements of the CO2 concentration in situ in order to
provide information for selecting appropriate CO2 sensor locations. They investigated a measurement system
in two buildings that used one CO2 sensor connected to a multiple tubing so that the air was sampled at
45 and 27 locations, respectively. Their ﬁndings provided helpful information regarding CO2 concentration
==PAGE:36==
18
2 Justiﬁcation
variations at walls in relation with the concentration measured in the exhaust air. The results indicated a big
importance of the source location on the obtained data. Nevertheless, the study focused on the performance
of existing systems and ﬁnding out a suitable sensor location, and not on the occupants’ exposure.
As has been noted, the above mentioned studies investigated the spatial distribution of the CO2 concen-
tration and the impact of ﬂow interaction on its dispersion. However, they did not take into account actual
exposure levels, i.e. to measure at the breathing zone of the occupants. The idea of ideal mixing in the entire
room is questionable, because presumably there is a higher concentration of pollution in the near zone of the
source. This also includes occupants as a source of metabolic CO2. The resulting exposure level is aﬀected
by the air ﬂow interaction, which is a highly complicated in the breathing zone due to the contribution of the
transient breathing process and the convective boundary layer (CBL) around the human body [30]. The CBL
develops due to a temperature diﬀerence between the body surface and the air surrounding the body. The
CBL moves the air surrounding the body upwards, entraining pollutants from the body and bringing it to the
breathing zone. On the contrary, the CBL is an important factor in displacement ventilation, when it brings
the clean air supplied close to the ﬂoor up to the breathing zone of the occupant. Inhalation and exhalation
also aﬀect the air ﬂow interaction in the breathing zone. In fact, Bivolarova et al. [30] described that the exha-
lation from nose and inhalation from the mouth can increase the exposure to pollutants emitted closer to the
breathing zone compared to the breathing mode inhalation from nose and exhalation from mouth.
Given these points, a higher concentration of CO2 is expected to be in the breathing zone since it is
respiratory-induced, than in locations in the room such as on walls [31]. This area, referred as the CO2 bub-
ble [31], is highly aﬀected by both static and dynamic factors. The static factors include body posture and
nose and mouth geometry, while dynamic factors include the occupant’s activity, indoor environment and
furniture geometry, and air movement in the micro-climate. Such a bubble can be displayed by using compu-
tational ﬂuid dynamics (CFD), as shown in Figure 2.1. However, the referred study did not specify in detail
the conditions applied for the CFD simulation.
(a) Inhalation
(b) Exhalation
Figure 2.1: Respiratory-induced CO2 concentration in the breathing zone creating the so-called CO2 bub-
ble [31]
Ghahramani et al. (2018) [31] focused on investigation of such CO2 bubble, its direct impact on exposure
level, and a possible way of suitable measurement of the exposure. One sensor location measured the con-
centration close to the nostrils, referred as the inhalation zone CO2 concentration. A wearable sensor placed
on the chest of the subjects was studied as a prospective device measuring the actual CO2 exposure. Conse-
quently, the results obtained by the wearable CO2 sensor were compared with the results measured closer to
the nostrils. The subjects were exposed to a typical oﬃce environment and asked to imitate common oﬃce
activities. The results showed that for 83 % of the cases, the CO2 concentration measured in the inhalation
zone was higher than the one measured by the wearable sensor on the chest. Therefore, a wearable sensor on
the chest is not a generalizable solution [31]. Nevertheless, the wearable sensor provided more representative
results for the background CO2 measurement than a wall mounted sensor. This study is further discussed in
section 6.3. Although the study focused on the CO2 exposure of the occupants, it did not comprise the impact
==PAGE:37==
2 Justiﬁcation
19
of numerous parameters such as diﬀerent air distribution, diﬀerent supply air ﬂow rate, various head posi-
tions, or the impact of presence of other occupants. It has not been clariﬁed whether the occupants mutually
aﬀect the level of the inhaled CO2, or if the inhaled CO2 concentration is aﬀected by only the person himself.
These parameters are expected to have an impact on the CO2 concentration distribution and consequentially
on the occupants’ CO2 exposure. Therefore, a further investigation of the impact of the parameters is needed.
As previously presented in section 1.2, there are numerous ways to deﬁne ventilation rate. Some of them
are directly based on the CO2 concentration in a room, others recommend using tabular values. Moreover, the
methods often aim at meeting the requirement on the CO2 concentration indoor. However, as it implies from
Figure 2.1 the exposure is considerably higher than the background concentration.
The standards mention that it is up to the designer’s judgment to decide what are the pollution sources in
the space. In case of the chemical load or the IAQ procedure in ASHRAE Standard 62, it requires to specify
the contaminants and the emission rates of each contaminant. Nevertheless, most of the methods are based on
sensory pollution from the occupants and the building component. It is the designer’s decision, whether these
two pollution sources are present or not. Table 1.9 and 1.10 showed that the diﬀerent approaches can lead to
considerably diﬀerent ventilation rates. While these methods aim at providing an acceptable PAQ, not all of
them succeed in meeting the CO2 concentration limits, as shown in Table 1.11. For that reason, the ventilation
rate based on the CO2 concentration should be calculated as well and the designed ventilation rate should be
the highest deﬁned value.
The ventilation rate based on the CO2 concentration as presented by CEN CR 1752 depends on the gener-
ation rate of the occupants, the outdoor CO2 concentration, and the required indoor concentration. The CO2
generation rate of occupants depends on their activity level and individual characteristics. CEN CR 1752 intro-
duces 19 L/h as the generation rate of a sedentary occupant. Current approaches for deﬁning the generation
rate have been assessed together with an impact of room air temperature on the CO2 generation rate of the
occupants [32]. The referred study implies that the current determination of the CO2 generation rate is overes-
timated. Instead it suggests an average generation rate of 7,89 mg/s. This value corresponds to 15,5 L/h. Based
on the current calculation methods for ventilation rates, this suggestion could decrease the ﬁnal ventilation
rate and consequently the dimension of ventilation systems.
The commonly used outdoor CO2 concentration is 400 ±50 ppm [25]. Nevertheless, the outdoor CO2 de-
pends on the location of the building (countryside, downtown, etc.) and a possible presence of local pollution
sources. ASHRAE Standard 62 thus recommends an observational survey of the building surrounding prior to
the ventilation design. The outdoor CO2 concentration measurements should be performed at the air intake
in order to obtain correct results [33].
Another approach for saving energy in buildings is the application of a demand control ventilation sys-
tem (DCV). Despite the ventilation rate, the DCV does not aﬀect the design ventilation capacity. The DCV can
lead to energy savings from 5 % to 80 % comparing to a ﬁxed ventilation strategy [34]. Nevertheless, the CO2
sensor performance and location are crucial for the correct and eﬃcient operation of the DCV. The quality
of CO2 sensors including their accuracy, response time, and reliability has been improving over time, driven
by the market and an increasing number of installed sensors [29]. However, as previously presented in sec-
tion 1.3, there are no deﬁnite requirements on the exact sensor location. The recommendations for current
practice are rather vague and do not distinguish between diﬀerent air distribution methods. The working
principle of air distribution methods implies that one sensor location in the room is hardly equally suitable
for all of the air distributions to measure and represent the actual occupants exposure. DCV usually controls
the concentration in the exhaust air or at a point in the room far from the breathing zone. Another issue that
needs consideration is the fact that the change of the supply ﬂow rate modiﬁes the air distribution in the room
and thus the exposure of the occupants. Moreover, placing the sensor in the area of clean air down ﬂow can
result in incorrect readings. The location of the occupants might also have an eﬀect on the CO2 exposure.
However, this is not considered when DCV is applied. Clear recommendations on the sensor location should
be provided with respect to the designed air distribution method, the type and location of the diﬀuser, and
the occupants’ location and behaviour.
==PAGE:38==
20
==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER3
Objectives
The objectives of the study are:
• to examine whether and to what extent CO2 exposure of room occupants diﬀers from CO2 concentration
in exhaust air;
• to investigate the eﬀect of diﬀerent room air distribution, heat loads distribution, type, number and
location of diﬀuser, and occupants’ behaviour on CO2 exposure;
• to examine CO2 concentration distribution in a room under diﬀerent air distribution patterns, and
strength and location of heat load;
• to recommend the most suitable calculation method of ventilation rate;
• to outline recommendations for appropriate CO2 sensor location in order to obtain desired CO2 exposure
level for occupants.
==PAGE:40==
22
==PAGE:41==
CHAPTER4
Methodology
Chapter 4 introduces the experiment conducted within this study. Firstly, the laboratory facilities, the climate
chamber, and the ventilation system are described, including diﬀerent air distribution methods and installed
diﬀusers. Secondly, the experimental conditions are presented together with the descriptions of the investi-
gated parameters. The labeling of the sessions with diﬀerent experimental conditions studied is introduced.
Thirdly, all measuring equipment used in the experiment is described in detail. The human subjects, the en-
rolment and their role in the experiment are presented, together with subjective questionnaires as a part of
the data collection. The schedule of the experiments, the daily procedure, the data collection, and the physical
measurements are introduced. Additional sessions and smoke visualizations performed during the study are
described. Finally, the applied data analysis is described.
4.1
Facilities
4.1.1
Climate chamber
The experiment was carried out in the laboratory facilities of International Centre for Indoor Environment
and Energy (ICIEE) at Technical University of Denmark, in building 412. The measurements were conducted
in Chamber 5. The dimensions of the chamber were 4,2 x 4,12 x 2,89 m. The control of the supply and ex-
haust air of the room was done by measuring the diﬀerential pressure at balancing dampers installed in the
corresponding ducts. The dampers were located in an adjacent control room with dimensions 4,2 x 1,1 x 3,1 m.
Figure 4.1 presents basic dimensions of the chamber. This speciﬁc chamber was designed to be customized to
various air distributions, such as mixing and displacement ventilation. It also includes chilled ceiling. Mea-
suring instruments and computers were located outside of the chamber, at the so called Climate square. There
were 4 energy-saving lights in the chamber, with 40 W power each. The lights were mounted in two rows
perpendicular to the artiﬁcial window.
Figure 4.1: Dimensions of chamber 5 and control room
==PAGE:42==
24
4 Methodology
Artiﬁcial windows and direct solar radiation
On the wall between the chamber and the control room, radiant panels simulating a window and solar heat
loads were placed. Those panels were supplied with hot or cold water to reach a desired surface temperature,
and thus to simulate summer or winter outdoor conditions. During the experiments summer conditions were
simulated (the conditions are described in section 4.3). The supply water temperature was controlled by a
separate system, shown in Figure A.1. Five radiant panels with total dimensions of 2,0 m x 0,8 m were overlaid
with an aluminium metal plate painted in grey to achieve higher emissivity coeﬃcient. The actual area of
artiﬁcial window was 6,3 m2, the rest of the wall area was covered by insulation. The artiﬁcial window is
indicated in Figure 4.1. It can also be seen in Figure 4.23. The power provided by the window was based on
the surface temperature and the room air temperature and it was calculated by
Qwin = 8(|ti −tS,m|),
(4.1)
where Qwin is the radiant power of the area (W/m2), ti is the room air temperature (°C), and tS,m is the
surface temperature (°C) [35].
To simulate the impact of direct solar radiation, electric foils were used. The foils were placed on the ﬂoor
near the simulated window as indicated in Figure 4.2. Insulation plates were placed underneath the foils in
order to direct the generated heat ﬂow to the chamber. The foils were overlaid by PVC ﬂooring, which was uni-
form for the whole ﬂoor area. The electric foils were connected to a voltage transformer with a potentiometer.
This made it possible to control the heat generated by the foils.
Chilled ceiling
The radiant chilled ceiling was 2,89 m above the ﬂoor. It consisted of 18 radiant panels with dimensions
of 1,2 m x 0,6 m, as shown in Figure 4.3. The middle part of the ceiling was made out of regular plasterboard
and three ceiling air terminal devices were located there. The total area of the chilled ceiling was 12,96 m2,
which was 75 % of the total ceiling area. This was only 5 % more than the percentage recommended by the
manufacturer [36]. Water with diﬀerent temperature and ﬂow rate was circulated through pipes built in the
panels.
The panels were jointed in six groups, connected in series as it is illustrated in Figure 4.3. The pressure drop
and the ﬂow rate in pipes were equalized by connecting the return pipe in a rotate loop. The cold water was
supplied from a main chiller and had temperature mode 7 °C/12 °C. These temperatures were too low for the
purpose of the experiment, so an additional system for temperature control was pre-connected to the chilled
ceiling in the chamber. The cooling power of the ceiling was controlled based on its surface temperature, given
by
Qcc = 8,92(|ti −tS,m|)1,1,
(4.2)
where Qcc is the radiant power of the chilled ceiling (W/m2), ti is the room air temperature (°C), and tS,m
is the surface temperature of the ceiling (°C) [35].
4.1.2
Air distribution methods
The ventilation system of the chamber allowed variable and quick change of the supply and exhaust branches.
The main part of the adjacent system was located in the control room contiguous to the chamber, where it was
easily operated, balanced and monitored.
==PAGE:43==
4.1 Facilities
25
Figure 4.2: Electrical foils placed on the ﬂoor,
marked in red
Figure 4.3: Radiant ceiling panels connection:
clod water supply (blue), warm water return
(red)
An HVAC system located in the basement of the building was used to transport air to the control room via
a ø160 main supply duct. From this main supply duct, three branches with diameter ø125 were connected to
the three ceiling diﬀusers, used when mixing ventilation was studied. A separate ø160 supply duct was used
for connecting a textile duct diﬀuser or a specially designed box diﬀuser, as described later in this section.
During the experiment, there was one extra branch ø125 added at the end of the main supply duct, leading to
a displacement diﬀuser. The same ducting was used as a second exhaust for the cases with the textile duct. All
branches were equipped with shut oﬀvalves and ﬂow balancing dampers. The photo-documentation of the
system is presented in Appendix A.
The air was exhausted through circular exhaust valves, placed either close to the ﬂoor (in the case of mix-
ing air distribution with diﬀusers placed on the ceiling, Figure 4.4), or in the corners on the ceiling (in the
remaining studied cases). Ducts with diameter of 125 mm were used to transport the air to the main ø160
exhaust duct. A fan located outside the chamber was used to exhaust the air. The exhaust air was released to
the Climate square.
Mixing air distribution
The mixing air distribution was achieved with three ceiling slot diﬀusers SLN 472 from Halton [37, 38].
The dimensions and the placement of the diﬀusers are shown in Figure 4.4. The diﬀusers were connected to
the ø125 ducting via plenum boxes made specially for the chamber due to the small space above the suspended
ceiling. Each ø125 branch had a shut-oﬀvalve and a balancing damper. Hence, the slot diﬀusers could be used
in various combinations with balanced ﬂow rates.
One exhaust circular valve diﬀuser was located on the wall under the artiﬁcial window, close to the ﬂoor.
This location was chosen to avoid a short circuit of the clean air supplied from the ceiling slot diﬀusers. The
ﬂow rate was controlled either by the circular valve itself, or by the balancing damper, or by electrical power
supplied to the exhaust fan. Mixing air distribution is also referred as mixing ventilation (MV) later in the
thesis.
Displacement air distribution
A semicircular perforated displacement diﬀuser with a large diﬀusion was located in one of the corners
opposite to the artiﬁcial window. The dimensions and placement of the displacement diﬀuser are shown in
Figure 4.5. The diﬀuser was CQA type manufactured by Lindab [39]. The diﬀuser was connected to the air

Figure 4.4: Set up for mixing air distribution
supply system from the top (Figure 4.5, VAL-2), through a ø160/125 reduction to a ø125 insulated ﬂexible
duct coming from the ceiling as shown in Figure A.3.
The exhaust air valve for the displacement ventilation was placed at the ceiling in the second corner (Fig-
ure 4.5, VAL-1) opposite the artiﬁcial window. This exhaust was blocked when the mixing ventilation was
studied. Displacement air distribution is also referred as displacement ventilation (DV) later in the thesis.

==PAGE:45==
4.1 Facilities
27
Ventilation with textile duct
Low impulse air supply from a textile duct (TD) was used to achieve air distribution diﬀerent than the
mixing and displacement air distribution. A semicircular textile duct with ø200 and connection of ø160 was
used. The dimensions and placement of the textile duct are shown in Figure 4.6. The textile duct was 3 m
long and was attached directly at the ceiling, with its impermeable side on the top, as presented in Figure A.4.
The textile duct was an inject hybrid system type of duct with a number of small holes in the bottom part.
The working principle of a low impulse system is based on a passive thermal displacement, by supplying cool
air with low velocity throughout the whole permeable surface of the textile duct. In contrast, the working
principle of a high impulse principle is based on mixing by supply of clean air at a high velocity through laser
cut holes or through nozzles [40]. Inject hybrid system comprises low impulse ducts which are made active by
using holes. The aim was to supply clean cold air above the table to drop down and be entrained by the free
convection ﬂow around the occupant’s body, and moved to the breathing zone.
The textile duct was connected to the ø160 supply branch (Figure 4.6), which end was at the wall above
the artiﬁcial window. The textile duct was attached to the plasterboard middle elements of the ceiling and
covered the slot diﬀusers at the same time. Two exhaust valves located on the two ceiling corners (Figure 4.6,
VAL-1 and VAL-2) opposite to the artiﬁcial window were used together with the textile duct diﬀuser.
Figure 4.6: Set up for ventilation with the textile duct
Ventilation with box diﬀuser
A special diﬀuser type designed as a rectangular box named box diﬀuser (BD) was employed in this study.
It was originally a prototype box with incorporated perforated quarter-circular textile duct. The textile duct
was attached around the box inner perimeter. The intention of this diﬀuser was to supply clean air directly
above the occupied zone close to the breathing zone.
The design of the diﬀuser is shown in Figure 4.7. A picture of the box diﬀuser is shown in Figure A.5 in
Appendix A. Clean air was supplied via a ø160 ﬂexible duct into a plenum, from where it was transported
with lower velocity to the quarter-circular textile duct attached to the box perimeter through a set of smaller
holes. This ensured low supply air velocity and uniform air supply distribution throughout the textile duct.

Figure 4.7: Dimensions of the box diﬀuser
Figure 4.8: Set up for ventilation with the box diﬀuser

The dimensions and placement of the box diﬀuser are shown in Figure 4.8. The box diﬀuser was located
above the table with its bottom edge at a height of 2 m. By smoke visualization it was veriﬁed, that this height
is appropriate for the designed ﬂow rate to penetrate the occupied zone. The aim was the same as the one
with the textile duct, i.e. to provide clean air closer to the breathing zone. The box diﬀuser was used together
with one exhaust valve (Figure 4.8, VAL-1) located in the corner of the ceiling, the same as in case of the
displacement ventilation.
4.2
Experimental set up
4.2.1
Layout
The aim was to simulate a typical meeting room with a common layout. A table for 8 people was placed in the
middle of the chamber, with 8 upholstered oﬃce chairs surrounding it. The table was placed with its short
edge facing the windows, as shown in Figure 4.9a. The main reason for this direction was to have the table
parallel to the textile duct. The size of the table was 2 m x 0,9 m. Seven persons and one breathing thermal
manikin (TM) were placed around the table as it is shown in Figure 4.9a. The persons were asked to sit upright
with their lower chest approx. 0,1 m from the edge of the table. In one session the size of the table was changed
to 2,8 m x 0,75 m and the persons and the manikin were seated as shown in Figure 4.9b. In one other session
with the wide table, the subjects were asked to sit with their lower chest attached to the table, i.e. no gap
between their body and the table. After all subjects were seated, their chairs were blocked at the ﬂoor level
in order to maintain the desired position during the whole session. The purpose was to study the impact of
blocking the free convection ﬂow in front of the body on the exposure. Figure 4.9 also presents a numbering
of the subjects, used further in the study.
(a) Wide table
(b) Narrow table
Figure 4.9: Types of table and diﬀerent arrangements with marked position of the thermal manikin (orange)
The location of the manikin during the experiments with the two tables is indicated on Figure 4.9. It
was closely surrounded by the subjects. The distance to the facilities outside the chamber was reasonable
regarding the length of the power connection and the length of the pipes to the artiﬁcial lungs. The posture
of the manikin was upright. The subjects were instructed to maintain as much as possible the same posture
during the measurements. Every subject had assigned seat for the entire experiment period. The subjects
were asked not to move the position of the chair during the measurement. The desired posture was upright,
nevertheless because of comfort they leaned backwards from time to time, preferably keeping the distance
between their abdomen and the table. During the sessions, subjects were allowed to read any written or
printed materials, books or papers, use small electronic devices, such as tablets, e-books or smart phones, and
==PAGE:48==
30
4 Methodology
use their headphones. The subjects were asked to keep their belongings on the desk, so that no paper or tablet
was blocking the ﬂow of the exhaled air.
4.2.2
CO2 measuring points
One of the objectives of this study was a detailed investigation of the CO2 concentration distribution in the
room under common working space conditions. To obtain adequate picture of the concentration distribu-
tion, measurements at twenty points were performed. The CO2 concentration was measured by two diﬀerent
instruments, described in section 4.4.1.
The key point for this research was to measure the inhaled CO2 concentration. The CO2 concentration
in the inhaled air was measured in the artiﬁcial lungs. The artiﬁcial lungs realistically simulated human
breathing process of inhalation and exhalation, while dosing an adequate amount of CO2 in the exhaled air.
The system is described in section 4.4.6.
Figure 4.10: Plan of the arrangement of CO2 concentration measuring points. The legend states the speciﬁca-
tion and the height of each point.
Other sampling points were located across the room. Two points were positioned at 0,5 m and 1,0 m height
above the table. The aim was to observe the concentration in the middle of the occupied zone and its relation
to the actual inhaled CO2 level. Measurements in the occupied zone at 0,6 m from the walls were done in six
points. There were two stands in total. The sampling tubes were attached to one stand in four heights (0,6 m,
0,8 m, 1,1 m, and 1,4 m), and to the second stand in two heights (1,1 m and 1,7 m), in order to ﬁnd possible
vertical stratiﬁcation of the CO2 concentration. The stands with speciﬁed measured parameters and heights
are presented in Figure 4.11. The locations of the stands and the heights are speciﬁed in Figure 4.10. The
concentration was also measured at the supply and exhaust air.
Moreover, CO2 measurements were performed at nine measuring points located along the walls, except
the wall with the artiﬁcial window. This way, the CO2 concentration close to the walls at diﬀerent height
was examined to address the recommendations in present standards with regard to CO2 sensor placement.
The CO2 concentration on the walls was measured in 1,1 m, 1,4 m and 1,8 m height. The arrangement of all
concentration measuring points is shown in Figure 4.10.

4.2.3
Temperature measuring points
The room air temperature, the supply and exhaust air temperature, and the surface temperatures were mea-
sured at 57 measuring points. The arrangement of all temperature measuring points is shown in Figure 4.12.
The speciﬁcation of the measuring points with their heights is given in Table 4.1.
The surface temperature tS,m was measured by sensors attached to the surfaces with a thermal paste and
covered by a small piece of insulation. The sensors at the north, east, and south wall were attached in two
heights (1,0 m and 2,0 m). The sensors on the ﬂoor were placed as presented in Figure 4.12. The sensors on
the ceiling and the artiﬁcial windows provided the desired feedback for controlling the power of the radiant
systems, as mentioned in section 4.1.1. The water temperature of the chilled ceiling system was measured by
temperature sensors attached to the pipes and properly isolated from the surrounding. It was assumed that
the pipe surface temperature is nearly the same as the water temperature.
Air temperature was monitored at both the supply and exhaust. The temperature of exhaust air was moni-
tored in the bottom and ceiling exhausts. One temperature sensor was placed in the displacement diﬀuser, in
each slot diﬀuser, and one in the textile duct and box diﬀuser.
The thermal conditions and prospective stratiﬁcation in the occupied zone were monitored by temperature
sensors in diﬀerent heights on two stands placed in the chamber. The stand behind the thermal manikin
(stand 1) had six temperature sensors at 0,1 m, 0,6 m, 0,8 m, 1,1 m, 1,4 m and 1,7 m height. The second stand
(stand 2) was placed behind subject 6 and the given heights on this stand were 0,05 m, 0,1 m, 0,2 m, 0,6 m,
0,8 m, 1,1 m, 1,4 m and 1,7 m.
Ten low velocity transducers were monitoring air speed, turbulence intensity, air temperature, and risk of
draft. On the stand behind the manikin (stand 1), the local air speed was measured at 0,1 m, 0,6 m, 1,1 m, and
1,7 m height. On the stand behind subject 6 (stand 2), transducers were placed at heights of 0,1 m, 0,6 m, 0,8 m,
1,1 m, 1,4 m, and 1,7 m. The schema of the stands with respective heights and the corresponding sensor type
is shown in Figure 4.11.

4.3
Experimental conditions
The measurements were carried out under 10 diﬀerent conditions in 10 separate measurement sessions. Ta-
ble 4.2 gives an overview of the conditions for each measurement session. In every session the air temperature
ti was kept at 26°C, which is the recommended design indoor temperature for summer season for category B
according to DS/EN 15251 [1]. The labeling of the experimental conditions is deﬁned in Table 4.3 and Fig-
ure 4.13.
As previously mentioned, one of the 8 occupants of the meeting room was a breathing thermal manikin
that simulated a person. The breathing cycle of the manikin consisted of 2,5 s inhalation, 2,5 s exhalation and
1 s break. In order to provide realistic conditions, the thermal manikin was also a source of CO2 itself. During
each session, 0,771 L/ min of CO2 was dosed to the exhaled air, which resulted in a CO2 generation rate of
9,66 mg/s. The calculation of the dosing rate of the CO2 gas in the exhaled air is presented in Appendix B.1.
This amount corresponds to a typical CO2 pollution load per occupant of 19 L/h as stated in CEN CR 1752 [2].
Since room air temperature was set to 26°C for each session, the heat balance for the diﬀerent conditions
was calculated. Respectively, the desired power of the artiﬁcial window and chilled ceiling was determined
based on the heat balances. Appendix B.2 includes descriptions of all sessions in terms of heat balance and
designed system powers.
