==PAGE:53==
4.3 Experimental conditions
35
The impact of additional asymmetrically located heat loads was investigated by implying solar load in
the chamber. The hypothesis assumed that extra heat power on one side of the chamber generated buoyancy
ﬂows that interact with the air ﬂows in the room. Subsequently, this might aﬀect the resulting inhaled CO2
concentration. In order to limit the extent of the study, the impact of solar load was a subject matter only in
case of mixing ventilation.
For the same reason, the importance of the frontal distance between occupants was investigated only with
mixing ventilation system. In 9 out of 10 conditions, a table with 90 cm width (wide table - WT) was used. In
session 4, it was replaced by table with 75 cm width (narrow table - NT). Diﬀerent types of table required a
diﬀerent arrangement of the subjects, as shown in Figure 4.9.
The distance between the table and the subjects’ abdomens was aimed to be 10 cm, as a reasonable distance
for comfortable position at the table. The subjects were instructed to keep the same distance from the edge
of the table in each session, so the conditions for thermal plums around their bodies are comparable. A gap
allows a continuous ﬂow in the boundary layer, so the warmed up clean air reaches higher velocity when
ﬂowing along the body. The distance of 10 cm was changed only in one session with displacement ventilation.
The subjects were asked to sit close to the table with 0 cm distance between the edge of the table and their
abdomen. Closing this gap by ﬁtting the torso directly to the desk interrupts this air ﬂow. As a result, the air in
the restored thermal plum does not reach comparably high speed. This fact is related mainly to displacement
ventilation. Displacement air distribution is based on the convective boundary layer around the human body,
which brings the clean supply air to the breathing zone.
4.3.1
Air ﬂow rate
The air ﬂow rate was controlled by the operating software of the HVAC system. Moreover, the air ﬂow rate was
additionally balanced by the balancing dampers in the control room. The air ﬂow rate was designed to keep
the CO2 concentration level in the chamber at 1 000 ppm (parts per million). This equals to 600 ppm above
the outdoor level, which corresponds to category B in CEN CR 1752 [2]. The air ﬂow rate was given by
q =
S
Ci −Co
= nocc
GCO2
3600 × Cc,i
,
(4.3)
where S is the CO2 source strength (L/h), Ci is the indoor CO2 level (ppm), Co is the outdoor CO2 level (ppm),
nocc is number of occupants, GCO2 is the generation rate of an occupant (L/s), and Cc,i is the CO2 above the
outdoor level (ppm) [41]. In the current standard [2], the CO2 generation rate of an occupant, with sedentary
activity corresponding to 1-1,2 met, is 19 L/h. A recent study has shown diﬀerent results. The average CO2
generation of a sedentary occupant found by the study was 7,9 mg/s. This corresponds to CO2 generation of
15,5 L/h per occupant [32]. The air ﬂow rate for the experiment was deﬁned by applying this lower generation
rate. The aim was to verify, whether such designed air ﬂow rate would meet the requirements of 1 000 ppm in
the chamber. The resulting ﬂow rate was 57,4 L/s, determined by
q = 8
15,5L/h
3600 × 10−6 × 600ppm = 57,4L/s.
(4.4)
This ﬂow rate equals to the air change rate (ACH) of 1,15 h-1. However, as mentioned above, the manikin’s
generation rate was set to a higher value, according to the generation rate stated in CEN CR 1752, to incorpo-
rate variations among people. If the above supply ﬂow rate was designed exactly for the assumed generation
rates (i.e. seven subjects with lower generation rate and one subject with standard recommended ventilation
rate), the design value would be only 1,5 L/s higher.
==PAGE:54==
36
4 Methodology
The ﬂow rate in session 5 with mixing ventilation was designed according to DS/EN 15251 [1], i.e. to re-
move not only the generated metabolic CO2, but also the generated pollution from the building. The designed
ﬂow rate for session 5 was 68 L/s, i.e. the ACH was 1,36 h-1, deﬁned as
q = nocc × qP + A × qB = 8 × 7,0L/s per occ + 17,3m2 × ×0,7L/s per m2 = 68,1L/s.
(4.5)
In this equation, q is the total ventilation rate of the room (L/s), nocc is the number of occupants, qP is
the ventilation rate for occupancy per person (L/s per person), A is the room ﬂoor area (m2), and qB is the
ventilation rate for emission from building (L/(sm2)).
4.3.2
Supply air temperature
Diﬀerent supply air temperatures were designed for the sessions with the diﬀerent air distribution method.
Air distribution methods are based on diﬀerent principles. Therefore, they are designed in practice to supply
air with various diﬀerence between the supply air temperature and the room air temperature. Five of the ten
sessions were performed with mixing air distribution, with a supply air temperature tsup of 18 °C. The supply
air temperature for displacement ventilation was higher than for mixing ventilation. Usually, tsup is 1 - 5 K
colder than the room air, typically 1 - 2 K in case of a normal ceiling height of 2,5 m [42]. Considering the
diﬀuser placement and sizes of the near zone, the supply air temperature was 22 °C.
Certain types of textile duct work on the principle of displacement ventilation, i.e. with lower air velocities
and relatively small diﬀerence of the room and supply air temperature. As mentioned before, the used textile
duct was an inject hybrid system type. The manufacturer recommends a temperature diﬀerence between the
supply and the room temperature of 3 - 5 K for buildings of category B [40]. In order to keep the experimen-
tal conditions comparable, the designed supply air temperature was 22 °C for both the textile duct and the
box diﬀuser. Lower supply air temperatures may cause thermal discomfort, especially when being supplied
directly at the occupants. Session 9 was an exception with supply air temperature of 18,5 °C. The purpose was
to investigate the eﬀect of lower tsup on the CO2 exposure with the box diﬀuser. The supply air temperature
was lowered to the point, when the desired conditions in the chamber could be maintained without the use of
the chilled ceiling.
The parameters of the supply air were controlled by the software operating the main HVAC system. The
actual values were measured closer to the chamber. One temperature sensor was placed in each slot diﬀuser
and displacement diﬀuser to measure the supply air temperature. The sensor in the slot closest to the artiﬁcial
window was used to measure the supply air temperature also in the textile duct and the box diﬀuser.
4.4
Measuring equipment
In this study, physical measurements were performed to check and control the ventilation system and the
conditions in the chamber. Furthermore, the physical measurements were used to evaluate the indoor en-
vironmental conditions in the chamber. The physical measurements included air and surface temperature,
air speed, airﬂow rate, and CO2 concentration. The instruments and method for measuring the physical pa-
rameters are described in the following. The calibration of the measuring equipment is described in detail
in Appendix C.
4.4.1
CO2 concentration
4.4.1.1
Multipoint Sampler and Doser INNOVA
The CO2 concentration distribution was of the main interest of this study. Therefore, it was important to
measure it at numerous locations and with suﬃcient sample rate during each session. The CO2 concentration
==PAGE:55==
4.4 Measuring equipment
37
was measured at 11 points by three sets of Multipoint Sampler and Doser INNOVA 1303 and Photoacoustic
Field Gas-Monitor INNOVA 1412i. The locations of the measuring points are shown in Figure 4.10. The
measuring points on the stands with their corresponding heights are presented in Figure 4.11.
The Multipoint Sampler and Doser INNOVA 1303 has 6 inlets of channels on the front-plate, each with
a solenoid valve. Tubes with length of up to 4 m were connected to the system through air ﬁlters, that kept
the samples free of particles. All channels merge into one channel inside the instrument. The sampling of
the air through the channels occurs consecutively. A three-way valve directs the gas sample either into IN-
NOVA 1412 for analysis, or through the pump to the waste-air outlet on its back-plate [43]. The concentration
measurement is done by the Photoacoustic Field Gas-Monitor INNOVA 1412i, shown in Figure 4.14. The de-
vice works on photoacoustic infrared detection method, so it is capable of detecting almost any gas, that is
absorbing infrared light. Moreover, the gas-monitor is ﬁtted with a special ﬁlter, which measures the water
vapor and enables to compensate for water vapour’s interference [44]. The monitoring system is operated by
a software, that allows to select various settings, such as the number of channels, type of measured gas, and
also graphically displays measured data.
Figure 4.14: Photoacoustic Field Gas-Monitor INNOVA 14122i [44]
The analysis of the gas concentration sampled by one channel takes a time diﬀerent for each set. According
to the device’s analyzing time and number of channels in use, the total sampling interval was calculated for
each set. The number of samples per channel per 3 hours sessions for each set is presented in Table 4.4. In order
to keep a reasonable number of samples per session, only 3 or 4 channels were used per each set. INNOVA
5 was sampling and measuring with only 3 channels at the following locations: inhaled air of the thermal
manikin, exhaust and supply air.

Breathing is a transient process that includes 2,5 s of inhalation and 2,5 s of exhalation, and a short break.
But INNOVA has a longer time for analyzing one sample than is the inhalation period. It was not possible
to coordinate the sampling and breathing. Therefore, the sampling tube could not be placed directly at the
mouth of the manikin. The analysis of the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air was done using a jar of 1 L
volume, as shown in Figure 4.15. The inhaled air from the thermal manikin was led through the tightly sealed
jar, with a small fan in it ensuring a proper mixing inside. The end of the air sampling pipe, used for measuring
the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air, was placed into this jar, as shown in Figure A.9.

4.4.1.2
Vaisala Transmitters
The CO2 level at the walls was measured by Vaisala GMW90 Series CARBOCAP© Carbon Dioxide, Temper-
ature and Humidity Transmitters. The Vaisala transmitters use advanced Micro-Electro-Mechanical System
(MEMS) technology to measure CO2 concentration and enables continuous long-term measurements. Every
Vaisala transmitter was mounted to the wall, integrated in a small case, that protected an attached HOBO ana-
log data logger. Measurement range of the Vaisala transmitter is 0 ... 5 000 ppm with the accuracy of ± (30 ppm
+2 % of reading) in temperature range of +20 ... +30 °C. Operating temperature of the transmitter is -5 ... +55 °C.
The VAISALA transmitters measured the CO2 concentration every 10 s. See the transmitter in Figure A.6.
4.4.2
Flow rate
The measuring of the ﬂow rates was necessary in order to control and maintain the designed experimental
conditions. Moreover, the ﬂow rates were adjusted by the balancing dampers, and by the circular valves in
case of exhaust ﬂow rate. The ﬂow rates on each supply and exhaust branch were measured by the use of
balancing dampers, shown in Appendix A Figure A.2. Those enabled ﬂow rate measurements based on ﬂow
nozzle principle. The nozzle creates a pressure drop, that varies with the ﬂow rate. Before the nozzle, the air
pressure in the pipe is p1. After entering the nozzle, the air converges and due to this the pressure is reduced
until it reaches the minimum cross section area - the throat. Close after the nozzle, the air reaches its minimum
pressure p2. The pressure diﬀerence (p1 −p2) was measured using a diﬀerential pressure sensor, speciﬁcally
micromanometer FCO510, shown in Figure 4.16.
The q is determined by the pressure diﬀerence according to Equation 4.6.
q = k
√
∆pm,
(4.6)
==PAGE:57==
4.4 Measuring equipment
39
where q is the air ﬂow rate (L/s), k is the K-factor, given by the manufacturer, and ∆pm is the pressure
diﬀerence (Pa). The K-factor depends on the dimension and the positioning of the damper. It is requisite to
mention, that the air ﬂow rate was controlled by the software operating the HVAC system.
Figure 4.16: Digital diﬀerence micromanometer FCO510
4.4.3
Temperature and relative humidity
In the experiment, both the air and the surface temperature were measured. The surface temperature was
measured to maintain the desired conditions in the chamber and to control the performance of the radiant
systems. The air temperature was monitored in the supply and exhaust to control the ventilation system.
Moreover, the room air temperature was measured to validate the conditions in the chamber. A hand-held
thermometer WIKA CTH6500 was attached to a stand to inspect the room air temperature ti in the chamber.
The thermometer is presented in Appendix A Figure A.7. The stand was placed in the occupied zone, its loca-
tion is indicated in Figure 4.12 (measuring point RT-58). The CTH6500 thermometer has PT100 probe with a
high accuracy of 0,03 °C for a range of -50 ... 199.99 °C. This thermometer can have 1 or 2 measuring inputs and
enables data logging. It can be powered from a power supply or by batteries. In this experiment, CTH6500
was not used for recording the data, but only as a control thermometer of actual room air temperauter. The
desired room air temperature was 26 °C during the whole experiment. According to the displayed tempera-
ture on CTH6500, small adjustments were done in the beginning of a session in order to reach this value. The
thermometer CTH6500 was also used as a reference measure for a calibration of temperature sensors and the
thermal manikin.
Surface temperature was measured by resistance temperature detectors (RTD) PT100, class A with accu-
racy of ± 0,2 °C. The sensors were attached to surface via thermal paste and covered by a small piece of rubber
isolation. The data were collected by 34980A Multifunction Switch/Measure Unit, placed outside of the cham-
ber, as shown in Figure 4.17.
Another set of RTD sensors was used for monitoring the air temperature in the chamber and in the supply
and exhaust air. The measured data were collected by 34972A LXI Data Acquisition / Data Logger Switch
Unit, shown in Figure 4.17. The sensors were placed in supply and exhaust diﬀusers, and on the stand located
40 cm behind the thermal manikin.
Air temperature and relative humidity (RH) in the chamber were measured by digital humidity sensors
Sensirion SHT31-DIS. Those sensors measure RH with accuracy of ± 2 % and temperature with accuracy of
± 0,2 °C in a range of 0 ... 90 °C. The Sensirion sensors were connected to a high-performance evaluation kit EK-
H4, that enables logging data from up to four sensors and is connected to a PC via a USB cable. The measured
values are displayed directly on the kit and simultaneously on a screen of a PC. Two kits EK-H4 were attached
to the stand behind subject 6, logging data from eight sensors in total. See the schematic of the evaluation kit
in Figure 4.18.
==PAGE:58==
40
4 Methodology
Figure 4.17: Data logger units - 34972A LXI Data Acquisition / Data Logger Switch Unit (top), 34980A Multi-
function Switch/Measure Unit (bottom)
Figure 4.18: Evaluation kit EK-H4 conﬁguration with connection to read-out software on computer [45]
4.4.4
Air speed
In order to identify the air movement conditions in the chamber, the air speed v was monitored by mul-
tichannel low velocity anemometer with 10 transducers attached to the two stands (stand 1 and 2). For this
experiment, ten transducers SensoAnemo 5120LSF were used. Each transducer includes the speed and temper-
ature sensor. The sensors are vacuum covered with special aluminium coating that increases their resistance
to contamination and decreases the eﬀect of thermal radiation on the accuracy of velocity measurement. The
transducers have measurement speed range of 0,05 ... 5 m/s with accuracy of ±0,02 m/s ±1,5 % of readings. A
group of up to four transducers was connected together with a transmitter, that was wirelessly connected to
a computer. A suitable software was used to measure average speed and draft rating, the average was deter-
mined after 5 minutes measuring.
4.4.5
Thermal manikin
A breathing thermal manikin with a realistic female body shape was used to simulate the dry heat loss of a
seated occupant. The thermal manikin generates heat similar to an average human body. Figure 4.21 shows
==PAGE:59==
4.4 Measuring equipment
41
the thermal manikin seated in the chamber. It is divided into 23 separate body parts, each one is controlled
individually. A pure nickel wire is wound on the surface of each segment and embedded in a strong and heat
conducting polyester and painted with a strong dull colour. The wires are wound densely, with only 2,2 mm
distance between wires. Every segment has its own micro controller. The controller calculates the power
supply and the temperature of the entire surface of the segment by measuring the resistance of the nickel wire.
The manikin is operated by the Manikin software [46], that oﬀers 4 types of control:
• no heat (used for calibration)
• heat up to a given surface temperature
• heat up to a given heat loss
• following a comfort equation.
The comfort equation control mode was used in this study. It is given by
Pi = Tdb −T
Rt
,
(4.7)
where Pi is the power (W), Tdb is the deep body temperature (°C), T is the actual temperature (°C), and Rt
is the thermal resistance (m2°C/W), by default 0,054 [46].
Although the thermal manikin’s pivotal function was to monitor the inhaled CO2 concentration, it was also
used to objectively measure the thermal comfort conditions in the chamber. For this purpose, the manikin-
based equivalent temperature was used. The equivalent temperature teq is deﬁned as the temperature of a
uniform enclosure in which a thermal manikin with realistic skin surface temperatures would lose heat at the
same rate as it would in the actual environment [47]. In practice [48], the teq is deﬁned by Equation 4.8 for the
entire body or 4.9 for each segment separately.
teq = ts −(P /hcal)
(4.8)
teq,i = ts,i −(Pi/hcal,i)
(4.9)
The variables in the equations are following: ts is the surface temperature (°C), P is the measured convective
and radiated heat loss during the actual conditions (W/m2) and hcal is the combined heat transfer coeﬃcient,
determined during calibration in a standard environment (W/m2). The subscript i stands for an individual
body part.
hcal,i = Pi/(ts,i −tair)
(4.10)
The ﬁnal hcal was calculated as an average of all hcal,i for each body segment for 5 ambient temperatures.
After the experiment, the measured data were analyzed and the teq,i was calculated by using Equation 4.9. The
calibration of the thermal manikin is described in Appendix C.
4.4.6
Artiﬁcial lungs
The thermal manikin was equipped with artiﬁcial lungs. The artiﬁcial lungs allow realistic simulation of
human-alike breathing cycle, with timers controlling the air pumps so they can simulate breathing. As already
==PAGE:60==
42
4 Methodology
stated, the applied breathing frequency of a sedentary person was 10 min-1 [17], i.e. 2,5 s inhalation, 2,5 s
exhalation, and 1 s break. The assumed pulmonary ventilation was 6 L/ min, which means 0,6 L/exhalation.
As mentioned, the exhalation lasted for 2,5 s, which resulted in an air ﬂow rate from the lungs of 0,24 L/s, i.e.
14,4 L/ min.
The device consists of air distribution system, humidifying system, tracer gas dosing system and system
for maintaining exhaled air temperature. For this experiment’s purposes, only the exhaled air temperature
was controlled, not the humidity. The exhaled air temperature was approximately 35 °C.
(a) Front panel
(b) Inside the box
Figure 4.19: Artiﬁcial lungs
The artiﬁcial lungs box was connected with the thermal manikin by two 10 m long tubes. Those tubes
were attached to connectors located at the waist of the manikin. One connector led to the mouth, the other
connector led to the nose of the manikin. This way enables 4 diﬀerent breathing modes, according to used
connection of the tubes:
• inhalation from nose, exhalation through mouth
• inhalation from mouth, exhalation through nose
==PAGE:61==
4.5 Human subjects
43
• inhalation from mouth, exhalation through mouth
• inhalation from nose, exhalation through nose
The nostrils have a smaller area (38,5 mm2 per nostril) than the mouth (158 mm2). Thus, the exhaled air is
released with higher velocity. The exhaled air was assumed to penetrate the boundary layer and possibly mix
better with the air in the middle of the occupied zone. Therefore, the second breathing mode, i.e. inhalation
from mouth and exhalation through nose, was chosen in the experiment. The working principle is described
in Appendix A.
4.5
Human subjects
In this study, seven subjects participated in the experiment, including 4 males and 3 females. The same subjects
participated in all sessions. Thus, the CO2 generation was kept the same or similar during all conditions. The
recruitment process started on 24th September with posting ﬂiers around the DTU campus, and in the end 84
people replied to it. An initial form was sent to those persons, asking about general information and the time
availability for the experiment.
The applicants were considered, as long as they did not have any chronic or respiratory diseases, they were
ﬂuent in both read and written English, and had valid Danish bank account. Based on their response, only
25 suitable applicants were invited for the introductory meeting on 10th September at the Climate square in
building 412. At this meeting, the invited applicants were introduced to the procedure of the experiment,
the instructions regarding the experimental procedure, and the planned schedule of the sessions. The details
of the experiment (correct posture during the experiments, allowed activities, planned sessions, activities
of the staﬀwhen present in the test chamber, etc.) were explained. The applicants were introduced to the
questionnaires to be used during the experiment and the way to answer the questions. The ﬂier, the initial
form, and the instructions for participants are presented in Appendix D.
Table 4.5: Basic characteristics of the human subjects

9,4
Based on their body characteristics (weight up to 80 kg, height up to 185 cm) and especially the time avail-
ability, seven persons were selected and invited to participate in the experiment. Table 4.5 presents basic
characteristics of the selected persons. Overall characteristics are presented in Appendix E.
==PAGE:62==
44
4 Methodology
4.6
Questionnaires
During the 3,5 hours sessions, the subjects were asked to ﬁll in 5 questionnaires. The questionnaires served
to subjectively evaluate the indoor environment. The main focus was on perceived air quality (PAQ), thermal
comfort, and body conditions. Some additional questions regarding the subjects’ actions before the session
and their clothing were included as well. Examples of the questionnaires are presented in Appendix F.
Questionnaire 1 was provided to the subjects during the acclimatization part, before they entered the cham-
ber. The questionnaire included questions on how the subjects commuted to the lab, what breakfast she/he
had and how they slept during the night.
Another four questionnaires were ﬁlled in during the time in the chamber, one questionnaire each 60 minutes.
There were six types of questions, including four diﬀerent scales [1]:
• acceptability scale - divided into two parts, in ranges from -0,01 to -1 and 0,01 to 1, representing "just
unacceptable" to "clearly unacceptable" and "just acceptable" to "clearly acceptable", respectively
• 6-point scale regarding odour - "no odour" 0, "slight odour" 1, "moderate odour" 2, "strong odour" 3,
"very strong odour" 4, "overwhelming odour" 5
• 7-point scale regarding thermal comfort - "hot" +3, "warm" +2, "slightly warm" +1, "neutral" 0, "slightly
cool" -1, "cool" -2, "cold" -3
• continuous scale - in a range from 0 % to 100 %
After entering the chamber, the ﬁrst questions focused on air quality. The subjects were asked about the
acceptability of the PAQ. This question was evaluated based on the acceptability scale. The subjects also an-
swered whether they had noticed any odor in the air on the 6-point scale, and on the continuous scale they
responded whether the air was fresh or stuﬀy, humid or dry. The second part of the questionnaire focused
on thermal comfort, asking subjects about their overall thermal sensation on the 7-point scale and the accept-
ability of this sensation using acceptability scale. Furthermore, the subjects were asked whether they feel hot
(warm) or cold (cool) at any of their body parts and prospectively indicate which part and how the sensation is.
This question was accompanied with a drawing presenting a sedentary person, to indicate the body part, kind
of perception and its acceptability. The drawing with the instructions is presented in Appendix D.2. At last,
the subjects indicated what their preference regarding air movement was - more or less air, or no change in air
movement. The third part of the questionnaire targeted at body conditions and performance using continuous
scales. Questions related to eye and throat dryness, headache, concentration and drowsiness, often termed the
sick building syndrome (SBS). In the end of the ﬁrst and the last questionnaire ﬁlled in in the chamber, an
additional question regarding the subjects clothing was added in order to check their usual clothing level and
possible changes during the session.
Finally, the last questionnaire in the very last session contained a space for the subjects’ feedback on the
experiment, their experience, satisfaction with the process, and general comments regarding the experiment.
The feedback of the subjects is presented in Appendix I.2.
4.7
Procedure
4.7.1
Schedule
The experiment was conducted from 18th September to 11th October. The ten sessions were performed on ten
separate days. The schedule of the experiment is presented in Figure 4.20 and Table 4.6. A preliminary session
with volunteers was carried out on 12th September, in order to investigate the CO2 build-up time and to test
the functioning of the installed equipment.
==PAGE:63==
4.7 Procedure
45
The sessions were planned for 3,5 hours each. All sessions were carried out in the same time of a day in
the morning. The sessions started at 830 on weekdays and at 900 on weekends. The ﬁrst 30 minutes were for
acclimatization and preparation of the subjects for the session. The acclimatization phase gave the subjects
time to calm down, to modify their clothing, and get used to the indoor temperature. The remaining 3 hours
the subjects spent in the chamber.
Moreover, additional two sessions were performed with volunteers from the ICIEE. Those sessions lasted
for 4 hours, but there was no acclimatization period and the volunteers spent 4 hours in the chamber, with
only a short break after 2 hours. The purpose of the additional sessions was to examine CO2 as an adequate
indicator of indoor pollutants. The additional sessions are further described in section 4.7.4.
On days between the scheduled sessions, physical measurements were performed in the chamber using
heated dummies as heat sources instead of the subjects. Those measurements served to investigate the envi-
ronmental conditions in the chamber, i.e. air temperature, air speed, and draft occurrence. Also, depending
on the schedule, the ventilation system was adjusted for the following session.

4.7.2
Preliminary session
The preliminary measurement took place on 12th September in the afternoon. Seven volunteers were asked
to spend 3,5 hours in the chamber, following the same instructions as the future subjects. The preliminary
session mainly served to observe the build-up time of the CO2 concentration. This was necessary to verify
whether the planned 3 hours sessions in the chamber were long enough to collect suﬃcient number of CO2
concentration samples. The precursory estimation of the build-up time was approximately 90 minutes. Also,
the preliminary measurement allowed to test the operation of all installed equipment, including the CO2
dosing into the manikin’s exhalation, the functioning of the artiﬁcial lungs, and measuring equipment. The
CO2 concentration was observed only by the INNOVAs.
4.7.3
Procedure of the main sessions
The day before each session, the desired conditions were adjusted in the chamber, i.e. the speciﬁc air distribu-
tion method, the air ﬂow rate, the balance between supply and exhaust, the power of the radiant systems in
the chamber, and the room air temperature. In order to simulate the heat loads given by the occupants, bulbs
with 75 W each were placed on the table, to reach and maintain the design room air temperature, as shown in
Figure 4.21. The thermal manikin was turned on.
In the morning before the subjects arrived, the actual conditions in the chamber were checked and adjusted
if needed, especially the air ﬂow, and the surface temperatures of the chilled ceiling and the artiﬁcial window.
==PAGE:64==
46
4 Methodology
Figure 4.21: Set up with the thermal manikin and the bulbs as heat sources before the start of a session
The INNOVA sets and the artiﬁcial lungs (without dosing CO2) were started 90 minutes before the subjects’
entry to the chamber. The paper questionnaires were sorted and placed in folders on the table in the chamber,
together with one two-way radio transceiver (walky-talky). The other walky-talky was outside used by the staﬀ
to instruct the subjects during the session. The VAISALA CO2 transmitters automatically started measuring
30 minutes before the subjects entered the chamber.
At 830 AM or 900 AM the subjects arrived at the climate square in front of the test chamber. They spent
30 minutes of acclimatization seated. They were reminded about the instructions (proper body position dur-
ing the session, etc.). The subjects ﬁlled in Questionnaire 1. The time schedule for the questionnaires is shown
in Figure 4.22. Before the ﬁrst session, all subjects were weighted and their height was measured. The subjects
were asked to use the restroom if needed, since it was preferred not to leave the chamber during the measure-
ments. They were provided with a 0,5 L bottle of water each. Just before the subjects entered the chamber, the
bulbs were taken out. In general, any extra presence inside the chamber before the session was minimized, in
order to keep the CO2 level unbiased. All temperature sensors started logging.
Figure 4.22: Schedule presenting times when the subjects were asked to ﬁll in the questionnaires (AC - ac-
climatization part, EXPOSURE - time in the chamber)
After the subjects entered the chamber, they sat on their assigned places, as presented in Figure 4.23.
The two stands with the sensors were located to their positions. At the same time, the dosing of CO2 in the
manikin’s exhaled air started. Once everything was adjusted, the staﬀleft the chamber and the subjects stayed
inside without changing their position for 3 hours. It was possible to observe the subjects and check their
behaviour and body posture through a small transparent glazed section on the south wall. After entering the
chamber, the subjects were asked to ﬁll in Questionnaire 2. They were instructed and reminded to ﬁll in one
questionnaire every 60 minutes.
==PAGE:65==
4.7 Procedure
47
Thirty minutes after the start of the session, when the conditions inside were stabilized, the thermal
manikin was started to log data of the heat power to each body segment and its surface temperature. The
air velocity was measured 4 times during one session, each time as a 5 minutes average. During the sessions,
the supply and exhaust air ﬂow rate were checked constantly.
Figure 4.23: Subjects seated in the chamber
When the subjects left the chamber after the exposure, all equipment was stopped. The CO2 dosing to
the exhaled air from the manikin was shut oﬀ. The obtained data were exported and collected. After each
session, the bottle used to dose the CO2 to the exhaled air was weighted to check the amount of remaining
gas. Usually the conditions inside were kept after the end of a session and used directly to perform physical
measurements. In that case, heated dummies were placed on the chairs instead of the subjects, as shown
in Figure 4.24. After all needed measurement and smoke visualization were ﬁnished, the conditions were
adjusted for the upcoming session.
Figure 4.24: Heated dummies placed around the table together with the thermal manikin during performing
a physical measurement
==PAGE:66==
48
4 Methodology
4.7.4
Additional sessions
When monitoring CO2 as an indicator of indoor air quality, a question arose, whether CO2 can be assumed as
an adequate indicator also for other pollutants appearing in indoor environment. Two additional sessions with
volunteers were performed to answer this question. The volunteers were employees or Master students from
ICIEE and the Civil Engineering department. Extra CO2 was dosed through perforated pipes placed in a board,
simulating an areal pollution source. The board was placed on the ﬂoor, in the north-west corner, as shown
in Figure 4.25. The session on 26th September was performed with MV-57-18-2-NS-WT, the session on 3rd
October was performed with BD-57-22-NS-WT. The speciﬁcation of the conditions is presented in Table 4.2.
Figure 4.25: Board with system of perforated tubes for simulating an areal source of pollution
The additional sessions lasted for 4 hours each and the procedure was following. After adjusting the con-
ditions in the chamber, the volunteers entered and were seated around the table. The volunteers were asked
to follow the same instructions as the regular subjects. Only INNOVA sets were monitoring CO2 levels inside.
The other parameters were not measured. During the ﬁrst 2 hours, only the CO2 in the manikin’s exhaled air
was dosed. After the CO2 build-up period, the steady state CO2 concentration was maintained for the time
period suﬃcient for accurate measurements. In the middle of the session, the volunteers were allowed to leave
the chamber to use a restroom. In order to maintain temperature and concentration levels, one volunteer at a
time could use the break, while one of the practitioners replaced him/her at the seat.
After 2 hours, the CO2 dosing from the board supply device started. The CO2 dosing rate was 0,017 L/s
and 0,034 L/s for the two additional sessions, respectively. In the ﬁrst additional session the dosing tube
attached to this board was partially blocked by the way it was connected. Before the second additional session,
this connection was checked, and the dosing tube was re-attached to the board. This resulted in the higher
dosing rate. The dosing rate was deﬁned based on mass balance performed after the sessions. This dosing
was constant during the remaining 2 hours in each additional session. In the end of the additional session, the
volunteers left the chamber. The data measured by the INNOVA sets were exported.
4.8
Physical measurements
After each session, a set of physical measurements (PM) was performed. The measurements served to assess
the environmental conditions in the chamber in terms of supply, exhaust, and air temperature, mean air speed,
turbulence intensity, and draft rate. The PM were performed for all main sessions, except the one with dis-
placement air distribution when the subjects were asked to sit close to the table (DV-57-22-NS-W0). The PM
==PAGE:67==
4.8 Physical measurements
49
were performed during time outside the main sessions. The aim of the PM was to validate the designed con-
ditions, to examine the air speed and the draft occurrence and to gain a better picture of the environmental
conditions in the chamber.
During the PM both the heated dummies and the thermal manikin were used. There were ﬁve seated
dummies and two additional heaters, simulating seven persons in total. The ﬁve seated dummies had a bulb
installed in each leg, head, and torso, where it was supplemented by a small fan. This way ensured a closer
heat generation and distribution in terms of creating a thermal plume around the dummy. The heat generated
by the thermal manikin was 55 W on average, and 80 W on average by each of the dummies. Figure 4.27 shows
the seated heated dummy. The supplied electric power to all dummies and heaters was checked prior to the
experiment, to get as close heat sources as possible to the real occupants. The heat load and buoyancy ﬂows
from the remaining two persons were simulated by the heaters, with 75 W each. The use of the heaters did not
reproduce exactly the buoyancy ﬂows from persons. However, it was assessed that this would not substantially
aﬀect the distribution of the physical environment in the chamber.
Figure 4.26: Arrangement of measuring locations for physical measurements
The measurements were conducted at eight locations in total - one location behind each occupant, at a
distance of 60 cm from the wall, i.e. at the edge of the occupied zone. The stand was located approximately
50 cm behind the heated dummies on the long side of the table (seats 1, 3, 4, 5, 7, and behind the thermal
manikin). On the contraty, the stand was located approximately 20 cm behind the heated dummies on the short
side of the table (seats 2 and 6). The locations for the PM are shown in Figure 4.26. Eight temperature sensors
and six velocity sensors were attached on a stand. The temperature sensors were placed at the heights of 0,05 m,
0,1 m, 0,2 m, 0,6 m, 0,8 m, 1,1 m, 1,4 m, and 1,7 m. The velocity sensors were placed at the heights of 0,1 m,
0,6 m, 0,8 m, 1,1 m, 1,4 m, and 1,7 m. During the measurements, the stand was moved from one measuring
location to another. The air temperature and RH were measured by eight Sensirion SHT31-DIS sensors, the
supply and exhaust temperature were measured by PT100 RTDs, and the mean air speed, turbulence intensity,
and draft rating were measured by SensoAnemo 5120LSF transducers.
4.8.1
Procedure of the physical measurements
The measurements were carried out the day before a session, when the aimed conditions were ready, or after
the session, before the conditions in the chamber were changed for the upcoming session. The dummies
were placed on the chairs. The small heaters were always put on the same chairs in order to keep the same
==PAGE:68==
50
4 Methodology
conditions for all measurements. The measuring started later, after the temperature and air ﬂow conditions
inside the chamber reached the steady-state levels.
At each location, the temperature and speed measurements were performed for ﬁve minutes. Then the
stand was moved to the following location and the practitioner left the chamber again. The measurements at
the new location started after a break of 5 to 10 minutes, when the conditions in the chamber were stabilized.
Figure 4.27: Seated heated dummy during physical measurement
4.8.2
Smoke visualizations
Together with the physical measurements, smoke visualizations were performed to observe ﬂow patterns and
air ﬂow interaction. The smoke visualization was usually done with the heated dummies in the chamber, but
in two cases it was performed with human subjects. The ﬁrst case was after the session DV-57-22-NS-W0. The
second case was after the additional session, performed with the box diﬀuser. A smoke generator was installed
on the ceiling of the chamber. The tube supplying the smoke was inserted in the supply main duct and sealed
around by using a duct tape to avoid any leakage of the supply air.
A stand with a camera was placed in the chamber to take a video of the visualization. Except the above
mentioned two cases, no person was present in the chamber during the visualization. A person present in the
chamber would aﬀect the air ﬂow interaction. Consequently, it would not be the same as during regular ses-
sions. The visualizations were performed under steady-state conditions in the chamber. The smoke generator
was controlled by a remote control, supplying a suﬃcient amount of smoke into the room. The camera was
already recording, taking a video of the smoke distribution inside. After the smoke dispersed, the camera was
turned oﬀ.
The smoke visualization was further performed to assess a suitable installation height of the box diﬀuser.
It aimed to show whether the ﬂow under the designed conditions reached the table and whether the designed
diﬀuser met the expected supply ﬂow distribution.
==PAGE:69==
4.9 Data analysis
51
4.9
Data analysis
The data measured by the three INNOVA sets, PT100 temperature sensors (measured data were collected in
two data-loggers), the two sets of Sensirion sensors, the velocity transducers, the thermal manikin, and the
VAISALA transmitters were exported in text ﬁle format and collected after each session. All obtained data
were analyzed using Microsoft Excel.
The data measured by the INNOVA sets were corrected according to the linear functions obtained from
the instruments calibration and converted from mg/m3 into ppm. The correction is further mentioned in
Appendix C.1. As previously described in section 4.4.1.1, the INNOVA sets sampled with diﬀerent intervals.
Table 4.7 summarizes the channels’ locations for each set.
Table 4.7: Channel locations for sampling CO2 concentration by INNOVA sets

The obtain data of the CO2 concentration from the sessions were divided into build-up and steady-state
periods. The time when the steady-state was reached was determined based on the result plots from each
INNOVA set. The end of the build-up period was deﬁned when the diﬀerence between the measured con-
centration and the mean concentration in the exhaust air was less than 30 ppm during the last 10 min of the
build-up. For the further analysis the mean CO2 concentrations and the uncertainties were obtained from the
steady-state part. An example of the measured CO2 concentration in time and its data sorting in the build-up
and steady-state periods is shown in Figure 4.28.
In this study, two ways of presenting the CO2 concentration results were used. The excess CO2 concentra-
tion above the concentration in the supply air was used to compare the results measured during the sessions.
The CO2 concentration in the supply was not the same for all sessions. The impact of ﬂuctuating concentration
in the supply air was eliminated by comparing the result as the excess concentrations. In cases where it was
more relevant, the absolute CO2 concentration was used. The absolute concentration includes the concentra-
tion of the supply outdoor air.
Various indices are used in practice to assess the performance of diﬀerent air distribution methods, both
in terms of their ability to exchange the air in the room and the ability to remove air-borne contaminants [49].
The index that was most suitable to reveal the objective of this study was the inhaled air quality index εc
P
deﬁned in Equation 4.11 [49]. The inhaled air quality index is given by
εc
exp = ce
cinh
,
(4.11)
where ce is the steady state excess concentration of contaminant at the exhaust air (ppm) and cinh is the
steady state excess concentration of contaminant in the inhaled air (ppm). Accordingly, εcoz, εc
P are air quality
indices for the occupied zone and the local air quality index, respectively.
==PAGE:70==
52
4 Methodology
Figure 4.28: CO2 concentration in exhaust - MV-57-18-2-S-WT
The results from the questionnaires were subject of descriptive statistics in Microsoft Excel. The mean
value, 25%, 50% and 75% quantiles, as well as maximum and minimum values were deﬁned based on the
response of the subjects to the questions. The number of only seven subjects was not suﬃcient to perform reli-
able statistical analysis. Therefore, an analysis on the statistical signiﬁcance of the diﬀerences in the compared
results was not performed.
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER5
Results
This chapter presents the results obtained in the study. First, the results regarding the measured CO2 con-
centration are described. The main focus is on the inhaled air concentrations, the concentrations measured
both close to the breathing zone and in the occupied zone, and the CO2 measured at the walls. The measured
CO2 concentrations are presented as the excess values, if other way is not speciﬁed. Second, the physical en-
vironment obtained in the chamber during the main sessions is presented, including both the air and surface
temperatures, and the air speed. Next, the results of the questionnaires are introduced as the subjective assess-
ment of the environment. Finally, the results of the physical measurements done after the sessions with the
heated dummies are presented.
5.1
CO2 concentration
The obtained conditions during the performed experiment are listed in Table 5.1 for each session. The de-
signed supply temperature tsup was reached with a maximum diﬀerence of 3 %. The measured exhaust air
temperature texh diﬀered from the desired 26 °C by max. 2 %. The room air temperature ti was measured at
the height of 1,1 m at the two stands in the occupied zone. The results of ti presented in Table 5.1 are the
average from the two values. Relative humidity (RH) was not a matter of control during the sessions and the
obtained range was 30 - 50 %. The mean supply CO2 concentration in all sessions was 426 ppm.

5.1.1
CO2 in the inhaled air
Figure 5.1 presents the absolute CO2 concentrations for all sessions with a comparison to the designed value
of 1 000 ppm [20]. The error bars show the total uncertainty of the measurements. The uncertainty of the mea-
surements is described in section 5.1.5. The total uncertainties for the mean CO2 concentrations in the inhaled
air and the exhaust were smaller than 21 ppm. The uncertainties were considered in the data analysis and re-
sults. However, the total uncertainties of the CO2 concentrations were smaller than the diﬀerences between the
measured values. For mixing ventilation (MV), and ventilation with the box diﬀuser (BD) and with the textile
duct (TD) the diﬀerence between the exhaust CO2 concentration and the 1 000 ppm limit was max. 4 %, with
==PAGE:72==
54
5 Results
4 cases exceeding the 1 000 ppm. MV-68-18-2-S-WT was an exception. In this session, the supply ﬂow rate
(68 L/s) was based on the calculation recommended by DS/EN 152 for category B [1]. Furthermore, Figure 5.1
shows substantially higher CO2 values in the exhaust air than 1 000 ppm under displacement air distribu-
tion (DV). The results are also presented as the absolute and excess CO2 concentrations in Appendix G.1 in
Table G.1.
Figure 5.1: Mean absolute CO2 concentrations in inhaled air of the manikin and in the exhaust air
Figure 5.2 presents the excess CO2 concentrations measured in both the inhaled air and the exhaust air.
Except for DV, during all other air distribution methods the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air was higher
than in the exhaust air. Under MV, the CO2 concentration showed a similar pattern in all ﬁve sessions, with
the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air higher than in the exhaust air. The smallest diﬀerence between
the concentration in the inhaled air and the exhaust air was 114 ppm in MV-68-18-2-S-WT, while the biggest
diﬀerence of 255 ppm was obtained in MV-57-18-2-NS-WT with no solar gains applied. The calculated inhaled
air quality index εcexp was 0,78, 0,70, 0,77, 0,81, and 0,81 for the MV sessions 1 to 5, respectively. The inhaled
air quality indeces, speciﬁed in section 4.9, are presented in Figure 5.3 for all sessions.
As expected, DV provided cleaner inhaled air compared to the exhaust air CO2 concentration. Nevertheless,
the inhaled CO2 concentration with DV-57-22-NS-W0 increased substantially by closing the gap between the
manikin’s abdomen and the table (case DV-57-22-NS-W0, Table 4.6). Figure 5.2 shows that for DV-57-22-NS-
W10 the diﬀerence between the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and the exhaust air was 271 ppm. The
εc
exp was 1,56 and 1,06 for DV-57-22-NS-10 and DV-57-22-NS-W0, respectively, as shown in Figure 5.3.
The box diﬀuser and the textile duct turned out to work similarly to mixing air distribution, according
to the results shown in Figure 5.2. The CO2 concentrations in the exhaust air with BD and TD were similar
to the ones with MV. The clean air was supplied closer to the breathing zone, but the momentum of the
supplied air from the BD and TD caused mixing in the space (as observed during the smoke visualizations).
Moreover, supplying the clean air to the breathing zone by BD or TD did not improve exposure to CO2. The
decrease of supply air temperature in case of BD increased the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air (session 9,
Figure 5.2) compared to the corresponding session with BD and 3,5 °C higher supply air temperature (session 8,
Figure 5.2). The diﬀerence between the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and the exhaust air increased by
253 ppm from BD-57-22-NS-WT to BD-57-18,5-NS-WT. The εc
exp for BD-57-18,5-NS-WT was 0,6. With tsup
of 22 °C, BD showed better results than TD. The diﬀerences between the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air
and the exhaust air were 94 ppm and 150 pmm for BD (with tsup of 22 °C) and TD, respectively. The εcexp for
BD and TD with tsup of 22 °C were 0,87 and 0,8 respectively (sessions 8 and 9, Figure 5.3).
==PAGE:73==
5.1 CO2 concentration
55
Figure 5.2: Mean excess CO2 concentrations in inhaled air of the manikin and in the exhaust air
Figure 5.3: Inhaled air quality index deﬁned for all sessions
The supply ﬂow rate in MV-68-18-2-S-WT represents the current design approach recommended by DS/EN
15251 [1], where the supply air ﬂow rate is designed to remove not only pollution by occupants, but also from
the building itself. Therefore, the designed ﬂow rate for session 5 was 68 L/s. This higher ﬂow rate provided
lower inhaled CO2 concentration than with the supply ﬂow rate of 57,4 L/s in the other sessions with MV.
Moreover, the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air in MV-68-18-2-S-WT complied with the desired limit of
1 000 ppm, as shown in Figure 5.1.
The build-up time of the CO2 concentration in all sessions was determined from time plots of the CO2
concentration in the exhaust air measured with the INNOVA instrument. The determined build-up times
for the sessions are presented in Appendix G.1 in Table G.2. The build-up was less than 1 hour for all sessions,
which is shorter than the preliminary estimation of 90 minutes mentioned in section 4.7. The shortest build-up
time was 38 min measured in sessions MV-57-18-2-S-WT and MV-68-18-2-S-WT.
==PAGE:74==
56
5 Results
Impact of heat load distribution
A great diﬀerence between the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and in the exhaust air was found when
no solar loads were applied, i.e. in MV-57-18-2-NS-WT. The concentration in the inhaled air was substantially
higher than in the other MV cases, with the inhaled air concentration being 255 ppm higher than in the exhaust
air. Also the measured CO2 concentrations in the occupied zone were higher for the case with no solar gains
than for the four other cases with MV. This fact is shown in Figure 5.5. This might be a result of a poor mixing.
When the solar loads were applied, the air ﬂow interaction was aﬀected by high velocities created closer to the
window by the buoyancy ﬂow. The issue is discussed in detail in section 6.1.
Impact of number and location of diﬀusers
The mean CO2 concentrations in the inhaled air and in the exhaust air obtained for the reference case
(mixing ventilation with two slot diﬀusers, i.e. MV-57-18-2-S-WT), were fractionally higher than the concen-
trations measured in the case with only one slot diﬀuser (MV-57-18-1-S-WT). The diﬀerences between the
CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and the exhaust air were 154 ppm and 178 ppm, respectively. Moreover,
there was no diﬀerence in the CO2 concentrations measured in the occupied zone with one and two ceiling
slot diﬀusers, as shown in Figure 5.5.
Impact of distance between occupants
The impact of the distance between the occupants was investigated by using two tables with diﬀerent
width. The narrow table shortened the front distance between the subjects by 15 cm (from 110 cm to 95 cm).
Consequently, the arrangement of the subjects around the table had changed, as shown in Figure 4.9. Fig-
ure 5.2 shows that the distance did not cause any change in the inhaled air concentration in MV-57-18-2-S-NT
compared to the reference case. The CO2 concentration diﬀerences between the inhaled air and the exhaust
air were 154 ppm and 133 ppm for the reference case MV-57-18-2-S-WT and the case MV-57-18-2-S-NT, re-
spectively.
Impact of occupants’ behaviour
The occupants’ behaviour includes number of aspects, e.g. their activity level, the posture, or the loca-
tion in the room. This study focused only on their position towards the table, when using displacement air
distribution. Displacement air distribution is based on air density diﬀerences and free convection caused by
heat sources. By closing the gap between the occupant’s abdomen and the edge of the table, the ﬂow in the
boundary layer in front of the body was reduced. Figure 5.2 clearly shows that the CO2 concentration in the
inhaled air was substantially lower in DV-57-22-NS-W10 (with gap of 10 cm between the subject’s torso and
the table) than the concentration in the inhaled air in the DV session with 0 cm distance from the table. For
DV-57-22-NS-W0 with no gap the inhaled air and the exhaust air concentration diﬀerence was only 40 ppm.
Impact of supply air temperature
A diﬀerent supply air temperature for one of the air distribution methods was studied, namely with the
box diﬀuser. The performance of the BD was studied with the design tsup of 22 °C and compared with a lower
tsup of 18,5 °C. When the supply air temperature was 18,5 °C, the chilled ceiling was not in operation. The
results in Figure 5.2 show a considerable diﬀerence, when the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air in BD-57-
18,5-NS-WT was 169 ppm higher than in BD-57-22-NS-WT. A possible explanation is presented in section 6.1.
5.1.2
CO2 concentration close to the breathing zone
Two measuring points at diﬀerent heights were monitoring the CO2 concentration above the table close to
the breathing zone of the occupants. The measuring heights were 0,5 m and 1,0 m above the table, which
corresponded to 1,2 m and 1,7 m height above the ﬂoor. The excess concentrations are presented in Figure 5.4
together with the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and the exhaust air.

For DV sessions, all measured concentrations above the table were higher than the concentrations mea-

momentary behaviour of the subjects, and the long sampling time of the measuring instrument. This factor is
discussed further in section 6.1.
5.1.3
CO2 concentration in the occupied zone
The distribution of the CO2 concentration was measured on two stands in the chamber at 6 measuring points
in total. Figure 5.5 shows the non-uniformity in the vertical distribution of CO2 at measured locations for the
studied cases. For MV, the mean CO2 concentration varied in a maximum range of 54 ppm (in session MV-57-
18-1-S-WT). The lowest mean concentrations were obtained for MV-68-18-2-S-WT as expected, because the
clean air was provided with a higher ﬂow rate. On the other hand, the highest CO2 concentration levels were
obtained for MV-57-18-2-NS-WT, corresponding to the higher CO2 concentration in the inhaled air under
these conditions comparing to the other MV sessions. Relatively good room air mixing was obtained with
the mixing air distribution in the occupied zone, except the session with MV-57-18-2-NS-WT. In sessions 1, 3,
4, and 5 (speciﬁed in Table 4.2), the average CO2 concentration measured on the stands was close to the CO2
concentration measured in the exhaust air with diﬀerence of 61 ppm, 51 ppm, 57 ppm, and 7 ppm, respectively.
Figure 5.5 supports the assumption of concentration stratiﬁcation in case of displacement air distribution.
With DV-57-22-NS-W10, the measured range of CO2 concentration was 214 ppm, with the minimum mea-
sured concentration at 0,6 m and the maximum concentration at 1,4 m (behind subject 6). Similarly, with
DV-57-22-NS-W0, the measured range was 274 ppm, with the minimum measured concentration at 0,6 m and
the maximum concentration at 1,4 m (behind subject 6). The distribution of the CO2 concentration in the oc-
cupied zone also shows that BD and TD worked similar to mixing air distribution principle. The change of
the CO2 concentration was 51 ppm and 90 ppm for BD-57-22-NS-WT and TD-57-22-NS-WT, respectively. The
range in BD-57-18,5-NS-WT was 135 ppm.
Generally, the mean concentration for almost all sessions (except with DV) was higher on the stand behind
the thermal manikin at 1,1 m and 1,7 m. Similarly, the mean concentrations measured at 1,1 m were always
higher behind the manikin than behind subject 6, except for DV. The highest mean concentration for DV was
obtained at 1,4 m behind subject 6. The results of the excess CO2 concentration in the occupied zone are pre-
sented in Table 5.3. The measured absolute CO2 concentrations in the occupied zone with total uncertainties
are presented in Appendix G.1 in Table G.4.

sensors placed on the artiﬁcial windows. The orientation of the chamber and the walls are shown in Figure 5.6.
The absolute CO2 concentrations at the walls with their total uncertainties are presented in Appendix G.1 in

Figure 5.6: Orientation of the walls
Figure 5.7 shows that the CO2 concentration varied at the walls for all investigated air distribution methods.
The results obtained with MV show that good mixing was not achieved in the entire room, only in the occupied
zone, as mentioned earlier. The mean CO2 concentration diﬀered between the measuring points at walls.
Regarding the orientation, large diﬀerences in sessions 1 to 4 were seen at the north wall, where the excess
concentration varied in a range of 287 ppm. On the contrary, the excess concentration at the south and east
walls varied in ranges of 143 ppm and 127 ppm, respectively (for sessions 1 to 4, speciﬁed in Table 4.2). The
ﬁndings for MV, BD and TD were similar, as BD and TD acted similar to mixing air distribution. For BD
and TD, the diﬀerences in the concentrations were larger at the north and south wall than at the east wall.
In the case with mixing air distribution, at the south wall the highest mean concentrations were measured at
1,1 m, except for MV-57-18-2-S-WT and MV-18-2-NS-WT. At the east wall, the highest mean concentration
was measured at 1,4 m during all sessions.
With DV, the expected concentration stratiﬁcation was not observed at all three walls. The CO2 stratiﬁca-
tion occurred only at the north wall, with an average range of 235 ppm. However, this range was substantially
smaller at the other two walls, namely 106 ppm and only 49 ppm for the east and the south wall, respectively.
The CO2 concentrations measured at the three walls are compared in Figure 5.8 for each height (1,1 m,
1,4 m and 1,8 m). These results showed that the concentration at one and the same height varied among the
diﬀerent walls. Table 5.4 shows the mean concentration at the walls in diﬀerent heights and the CO2 concen-
trations in the inhaled air and the exhaust air. The presented mean values are an average of concentrations
measured at three walls at one height. Generally, the obtained concentrations at the walls were closer to the
concentration in the exhaust air compared to the inhaled air concentration, except for DV.
The highest mean CO2 concentration measured in the room was for most of the sessions at 0,5 m above
the table. This was mainly caused by the location of the measuring point, that was close to the exhaled air
of the occupants. This fact was mentioned earlier, and it is further discussed in section 6.1. The exception
was session DV-57-22-NS-W10 with the highest concentration measured at the north wall at 1,8 m height, and
session TD-57-22-NS-WT with the highest concentration at 1,1 m behind the thermal manikin. In the sessions
with the box diﬀuser the lowest measured CO2 concentration was at 1,0 m above the table, while the highest
concentration in the chamber was beneath this point, at 0,5 m above the table. After excluding the measured
concentrations above the table, the locations of the highest measured CO2 concentrations varied between the
air distribution methods. The high concentrations with mixing air distribution were measured at 1,7 m height
behind the thermal manikin. With displacement ventilation the highest concentrations were measured at
1,8 m height at the north wall. In the sessions with BD and TD, the highest CO2 concentrations were found at
1,1 m height behind the thermal manikin.

5.1.5
The uncertainty of the mean CO2 concentrations
For the presented data regarding the CO2 concentration, the uncertainty of measurements was deﬁned. The
total uncertainty comprises random and systematic uncertainty of measurements. The random uncertainty UR
is caused by random errors. Random errors are statistical ﬂuctuations in the measured data, normally caused
by the precision limitations of the measuring device. The random uncertainty can be reduced by taking more
samples. The random uncertainty is given by
UR∗=
√
Σ(x −x)2
(n −1) × n = SD
√n ,
(5.1)
where UR∗is the random uncertainty (-), x is the single measurement, x is the average of measurements,
n is the number of measurements, and SD is the standard deviation. The percentage value of the random
uncertainty UR can be calculated as
UR = UR∗
x
× 100.
(5.2)
The systematic uncertainty US is related to systematic errors. Systematic errors are reproducible inaccura-
cies due to for example instrument errors. The systematic uncertainty of the equipment was deﬁned after the
calibration according to the used reference gases to be 1,3 %, given by
US =
√
U2
rg1 + U2
rg1 + U2
rg1 + U2
rg2,
(5.3)
where US is the systematic uncertainty (%), Urg1 is the accuracy of the three of the reference gases (%), and
Urg2 is the accuracy of the forth reference gas (%) used for the calibration of the instruments. The accuracy
was 0,5 % and 1,0 % for Urg1 and Urg2, respectively. Finally, the total uncertainty UT , based on both above
mentioned parameters, is given by

(5.4)
The total uncertainties were deﬁned for all obtained results of CO2. The input data and the resulting
UT are presented in Appendix H. The appendix contains calculation of the measurement uncertainty for all
mean CO2 concentrations, i.e. mean concentrations from 20 measuring points per each session. Each line in
the tables includes following data:
• mean concentration
• standard deviation
• number of samples used to calculate the mean concentration
• random uncertainty
• accuracy of the two reference gases
• systematic uncertainty
• total uncertainty
The total uncertainty was in the range of 1,32 % - 11,46 %, corresponding to the range of 6 ppm to 154 ppm.
The highest uncertainties occurred for the CO2 concentration measured at 0,5 m above the table.
5.1.6
CO2 as an indicator for other air pollutants
The correctness of the assumption of CO2 as an adequate indicator of pollution in indoor air was checked
by two additional experimental sessions. The procedure was previously described in section 4.7. The aim
was to add another pollution source in the chamber and measure the performance of the ventilation with MV
and BD. The other pollution source, besides the CO2 generated by the occupants, was an extra dose of CO2
gas discharged through a board simulating an areal source. The board was placed on the ﬂoor in the corner
below the artiﬁcial window. The increase of the CO2 concentration in both the inhaled air and the exhaust air
measured during the additional dosing of CO2 were compared to the CO2 levels obtained during the ﬁrst half
of the additional session when additional CO2 was not generated in the chamber. It should be noted that the
two additional sessions cannot be compared with each other. A diﬀerent dosing rates of the CO2 through the
board were used in these sessions.
The concept whether metabolic CO2 is a good indicator or not for exposure to other pollutants in rooms
is illustrated in Figure 5.9. Figure 5.9 is only an example showing possible outcomes of the investigation. The
basic consideration was that if the concentration increases equally in the inhaled air and the exhaust air, a
proper mixing is achieved, and metabolic CO2 can be assumed as an adequate indicator. This case is shown
in the example in Figure 5.9a. If the concentration does not increase proportionally and it is higher in the
exhaust than in the inhaled air, the extra pollution is discharged without mixing with the ambient air, as
shown in Figure 5.9b. In this case the metabolic CO2 is not the proper indicator for pollution in the indoor air.
Figure 5.10a presents the results from the ﬁrst additional session performed with MV-57-18-2-NS-WT. The
extra CO2 dosing rate was 0,0174 L/s. The measured concentration without pollution represents the mean
CO2 concentration before dosing extra CO2. The values on top of the column bars in Figure 5.10 are the total
mean CO2 concentrations measured during the steady state with dosing the extra CO2. The CO2 pollution
in the graph is the diﬀerence between the measured concentrations in the steady state before and after the
dosing. The results show that the diﬀerence between the concentration in the inhaled air and the exhaust
air was 108 ppm and 51 ppm without and with the extra CO2, respectively. The concentration in the inhaled
==PAGE:83==
5.2 Environmental conditions in the chamber
65
(a) Equal amount of pollution detected in the inhaled
air and the exhaust
(b) Higher amount of pollution detected in the exhaust
air than in the inhaled air
Figure 5.9: Example of results with additional pollution measured in the inhaled air and the exhaust air
air increased by 34 %, while the concentration in the exhaust air increased by 49 %. This shows that the
concentration increase was not the same. This result suggests that even with MV, the metabolic CO2 might not
be a good representative for other room air pollutants.
Figure 5.10b shows the results of the second additional session with conditions of BD-57-22-NS-WT. The
extra CO2 dosing rate was 0,0342 L/s. The diﬀerence between the concentration in the inhaled air and the
exhaust air was 292 ppm without the extra CO2. On the contrary, the diﬀerence between the concentration
in the inhaled air and the exhaust air was only 21 ppm with the extra CO2. The concentration in the inhaled
air increased by 35 %, while the concentration in the exhaust air increased by 90 % after dosing the extra CO2.
This implies, that the extra CO2 was not mixed suﬃciently with the rest of the air. Nevertheless, the idea of
the BD was to provide clean air close to the breathing zone of the occupants, and thus to dilute the pollutants
in the occupied zone. These results show that in a room ventilated with a system other than complete mixing
air distribution, the metabolic CO2 is most likely not a good indicator for other room air pollutants.
5.2
Environmental conditions in the chamber
During all sessions with the subjects, the room air temperature ti and the air speed v were measured by
sensors placed on two stands. The stands were positioned at two locations in the occupied zone, as shown in
Figure 4.12. The air temperature and the air speed were measured in order to evaluate the physical conditions
during the sessions. Together with the draft rating (DR), they were also used to evaluate if the rooms thermal
environment met the standard requirements for thermal comfort. All results were compared to the limits for
indoor environment of category B in CEN CR 1752 [2]. The objective assessment of the thermal comfort was
additionally done by determining the equivalent temperature of the thermal manikin.
The results of the measured supply and exhaust air temperature and the RH in the chamber were previously
presented in Table 5.1. The surface temperatures measured during the sessions are presented in Table G.5 and
G.6 in Appendix G.1.

5.2.1
Air temperature
The room air temperature was measured by 14 sensors on two stands in total. The heights on which the
sensors were attached are presented in Figure 4.11. Figure 5.11 shows the resulting room air temperature ti
in all sessions measured behind the thermal manikin and subject 6. Figure 5.11a presents ti obtained with
mixing ventilation. An ideal mixing would result in an equal room air temperature in all measuring points,
which cannot be seen in the results. The room air temperature during each MV session varied within the
range of 0,8 °C. The accuracy of the temperature sensors was ± 0,2 °C. The temperatures at the bottom heights
behind the thermal manikin were lower than at the higher locations, because of the supply jet direction and
the throw length. In the sessions with BD and TD the room air temperature distribution was more similar to
the one in the sessions with mixing air distribution, as presented in Figure 5.11b. The lowest temperatures in
BD-57-18,5-NS-WT and TD-57-22-NS-WT were measured at the height of 0,05 m behind subject 6.
The room air temperature with DV followed the ventilation principle of stratiﬁcation, as shown in Fig-
ure 5.11b. The maximal room air temperature diﬀerence was 2,1 °C and 1,8 °C in DV-57-22-NS-W10 and
DV-57-22-NS-W0, respectively. In case of BD and TD sessions a more uniform room air temperature was
measured behind the thermal manikin than behind subject 6.
Although the measured parameter was the room air temperature instead of the operative temperature, the
acquired results were compared to the operative temperature criteria8. The highest room air temperature
was measured at the height of 1,7 m, except for BD-57-22-NS-WT. In BD-57-22-NS-WT, the highest room air
temperature was measured at 0,8 and 1,1 m on the stand behind subject 6. The lowest room air temperature
for all sessions was measured mainly at the height of 0,05 m behind subject 6, except for BD-57-22-NS-WT.
In BD-57-22-NS-WT, the lowest room air temperature was measured at 0,6 m and 0,8 m on the stand behind
the thermal manikin. To conclude, the lowest room air temperatures were measured at the lower heights (up
to 0,6 m). The room air temperature increased with a height, even in the sessions with BD and TD. Table 5.5
presents the diﬀerence between the average room air temperatures measured on the stands and the designed
room air temperature of 26 °C. Only in three cases the diﬀerence was larger than the accuracy of the temper-
ature sensors. While with mixing air distribution a uniform room air temperature distribution is expected, it

5.2.2
Air speed
The air speed was measured at six diﬀerent heights behind subject 6 and at four heights behind the thermal
manikin. The presented data are mean values from four measurements during each session. The results are
shown in Figure 5.12.
Figure 5.12a shows the air speed measured during the sessions with mixing air distribution. The air speed
measured behind subject 6 was more uniform along the diﬀerent heights. A considerably higher air speed than
0,2 m/s recommended by the standard for category B [2] was observed close to the ﬂoor behind the thermal
manikin with MV-57-18-2-S-WT, MV-57-18-2-S-NT and MV-68-18-2-S-WT. This was caused by the supply air
jets from the ceiling diﬀusers. The higher speed occurred at 0,1 m because the throw length of the ceiling
diﬀusers was substantially large.
As expected, a substantial diﬀerence in the air speed was measured with DV. However, this diﬀerence
occurred more behind subject 6. Simultaneously, the air speed at the higher points behind subject 6 was lower
than 0,05 m/s. The air speed behind the thermal manikin was nearly uniform within DV. An opposite trend
can be seen for BD and TD, where the air speed was more uniform behind subject 6. This could be the result
of the orientation and placement of the diﬀusers above the table. With BD, the highest speed was measured at
1,1 m. This was caused by the redirection of the clean air after hitting the table, discussed later in section 6.1.
The design criterion for a maximum mean air velocity for a conference room of category B is 0,22 m/s for
cooling season [2]. This requirement was met in all sessions, except at 0.1 m above the ﬂoor in the sessions
MV-57-18-2-S-WT, MV-57-18-2-S-NT and MV-68-18-2-S-WT.
Draft rating DR is a one of the indices to evaluate thermal comfort. It is described in CEN CR 1752 [2]
as a percentage of people predicted to be dissatisﬁed due to draft. The standard also sets the limits of DR
corresponding to the three categories, where category B requires DR less than 20 %. This requirement was
met during all sessions. The highest average DR was 19 % in MV-57-18-2-S-NT, found at 0,1 m height.
5.2.3
Equivalent temperature
To objectively evaluate the thermal comfort conditions, the equivalent temperature can be used. The calibra-
tion and determination of the equivalent temperature were described in detail in section 4.4.5. The equivalent
temperature teq was compared to the designed room air temperature of 26 °C and the resulting ∆teq,26,i was
given by the equation
∆teq,26,i = teq,i −ti = teq,i −26◦C,
(5.5)
8This comparison is allowed by DS/EN 13779 [18]. It states that in most cases the average room air temperature can be used as the
design temperature.

where teq,i is the equivalent temperature of an individual body segment (°C), and ti is the designed room
air temperature (°C). Positive value of ∆teq,26,i indicates that the body part felt warmer than 26 °C. On the
contrary, the negative values indicate that the corresponding body parts fet colder than 26 °C. The results of
the equivalent temperature for all sessions are presented in Figure G.4, G.5, G.6 and G.7 in Appendix G.2.
Figure 5.13: Change of the equivalent temperature ∆teq,26,i for the manikin’s body segments and whole body
for the sessions MV-57-18-2-S-WT and MV-57-18-2-NS-WT
Figure 5.13 presents a comparison of ∆teq,26,i for the cases MV-57-18-2-S-WT and MV-57-18-2-NS-WT. The
impact of the solar loads from the artiﬁcial windows can be observed for the upper body. For the face, hand,
forearm, and upper arm, the left segment showed a higher teq,26,i than the right segment. This was probably
caused by the position of the manikin in the chamber, with the artiﬁcial window on its left side. As mentioned
in the previous section, the air speed was substantially higher at the ankle height with MV. This fact can also
be noticed for the equivalent temperature. In four sessions with MV, the ankles and lower legs were colder
than the rest of the body. The last value presented in the results stands for the whole body ("All"), i.e. the area
weighted average of the all body segments. In all MV session, teq,26 of the whole body was higher than 26 °C,
namely by maximum of 0,6 °C.
With DV, the equivalent temperature for the feet and lower legs was colder, corresponding to the impact
of the lower supply air temperature and the higher air speed close to the ﬂoor. Figure 5.14 presents a compar-
ison of DV-57-22-NS-W10 with the reference case MV-57-18-2-S-WT (speciﬁed in Table 4.2 and 4.3). In both
sessions, the feet and lower legs were cold and they were even colder within DV than with MV. The equivalent
temperature for the rest of the body, except the back, was warmer than 26 °C within DV. Moreover, the equiv-
alent temperature of the whole body was higher than 26 °C for both DV sessions, namely by 0,6 °C and 1,0 °C
for DV-57-22-NS-W10 and DV-57-22-NS-W0, respectively.
An opposite trend was observed with BD and TD, where the upper body was colder than the lower body.
Figure 5.15 presents the comparison of BD-57-22-NS-WT and BD-57-18,5-NS-WT. It can be clearly seen that
the lower supply air temperature caused a substantially lower teq,26,i for the upper body segments. The lowest
equivalent temperature was found on the hands, because they were placed on the table right beneath the
diﬀuser. BD-57-18,5-NS-WT was the only session with an equivalent temperature for the whole body lower
than 26 °C.
