5.3
Perception of the environment by the occupants
This section describes the results of the subjective assessment of the indoor environment by the subjects. The
subjects ﬁlled in ﬁve questionnaires in total for each session, four during the time in the chamber. Those four
questionnaires are identiﬁed in the following text as Q2, Q3, Q4 and Q5. The questionnaires were divided into
three parts regarding perceived air quality PAQ, thermal comfort and sick building syndrome (SBS) symptoms.
It is important to note that seven subjects were not enough to allow the derivation of statistically reliable
dependencies. The results from the questionnaires served for a basic evaluation of possible trends, but more
samples are necessary to provide a statistically strong outcome. An example of the questionnaire is presented
in Appendix F.
==PAGE:90==
72
5 Results
5.3.1
Perceived air quality (PAQ)
The perceived air quality PAQ was in the acceptable part of the scale for all the sessions. The results of
the subjective questionnaires did not indicate any clear relation between the measured CO2 concentration
in the inhaled air and the PAQ. The PAQ was compared with the absolute CO2 concentrations (presented in
Figure 5.1). The comparison of the concentrations in the inhaled air showed a diﬀerence of 157 ppm between
the inhaled CO2 concentration in the reference case MV-57-18-2-S-WT and during MV-57-18-2-NS-WT, as
shown in Figure 5.1. However, this deterioration was not obvious from the results of the PAQ, as presented
in Figure 5.16. Even for MV-68-18-2-S-WT with the lowest measured CO2 concentration in the inhaled air
among the sessions with mixing air distribution, the PAQ remained similar.
When considering all sessions irrespective to the air distribution, the biggest diﬀerence in the absolute
inhaled air concentration was between DV-57-22-NS-W10 and BD-57-18,5-NS-WT with the diﬀerence of
405 ppm. Nevertheless, the subjects’ evaluation of the PAQ showed the opposite trend. The median of the
PAQ assessment was relatively high and in a narrow range for both BD sessions, disregard the measured con-
centrations in the inhaled air. Figure 5.16 shows the eﬀect of adaptability, since the PAQ improved in time
for all sessions. The mean acceptability of PAQ in Q2 was 69 % for the mixing air distribution, 70 % for the
displacement air distribution, 83 % for the ventilation with the box diﬀuser, and 78 % for the ventilation with
the textile duct. The average acceptability of PAQ in Q3, Q4, and Q4 together was 82 % for the mixing air
distribution, 82 % for the displacement air distribution, 90 % for the ventilation with the box diﬀuser, and
90 % for the ventilation with the textile duct.
Figure 5.16: Perceived air quality. Median, expected value 25 % and 75 % intervals and whiskers for the seven
subjects are shown in ﬁgure. 0,01 = just acceptable, 1,0 = clearly acceptable
The mean perception of air freshness was between 30 % and 70 % for all sessions, indicating that the sub-
jects found the air neither stuﬀy nor fresh. Figure 5.17 shows that subjects assessed the air slightly fresher for
MV-68-18-2-S-WT. Moreover, they perceived the air fresher in case of both BD sessions than for DV-57-22-NS-
W10 with the lowest measured CO2 concentration in the inhaled air.
The subjects’ mean assessment of the odour perception was less than 1 (= slight odour) for all measured
sessions, as shown in Figure I.1. There was no clear improvement of the odour perception for MV-68-18-2-S-
WT, when more supply air was provided. For all sessions, the subjects assessed the air as neither too dry nor
too humid, as presented in Figure I.2. The results of both the odour and humidity perception are presented in
Appendix I.1.

5.3.2
Thermal comfort
The subjects modiﬁed their clothing according to the room air temperature to the level of 0,52 clo on average.
The upholstered oﬃce chair on which they were sitting provided extra 0,20 clo. The results of the thermal
sensation of the whole body and its acceptability are shown in Figure 5.18 and 5.19. The median of the thermal
sensation of the whole body was between slightly warm and neutral for MV and DV. In the sessions with BD
and TD the whole body thermal sensation was closer to neutral and slightly cool, as shown in Figure 5.18. This
fact resulted in elevated acceptability of the whole body thermal comfort, as presented in Figure 5.19. Despite
the fact that the thermal comfort acceptability votes were more spread than in case of the PAQ, the trend was
similar. A relatively better thermal acceptability was reported with BD and TD, compared to the rest of the
sessions. Figure 5.19 shows that the mean of the thermal comfort acceptability was always above 70 %, except
for Q2 in sessions 1, 2, 6, and 7. The mean acceptability of the whole body thermal sensation in Q2 was
46 % in session MV-57-18-2-S-WT (session 1), 69 % in session MV-57-18-2-NS-WT (session 2), 66 % in session
DV-57-22-NS-W10 (session 6), and 68 % in session DV-57-22-NS-W0 (session 7).
A higher air speed was measured at the ankle height with MV and DV, as reported in section 5.2. Also,
teq,26,i was mostly negative for the low body segments in these sessions, except the session MV-57-18-1-S-WT.
The subjects reported cold local sensation at the lower legs or ankles in 21 % of the votes in MV-57-18-1-S-WT.
The percentage is derived from the total number of answers to the question throughout one session (7 answers
per each question in one questionnaire, 28 answers per session in total). Despite the air speed results from
Figure 5.12a, none of the subjects felt cold at their lower legs or ankles in MV-57-18-2-NS-WT. Unlike the
equivalent temperature of the manikin with DV, the subjects reported hot local sensation at their lower legs
or ankles. The percentage of votes for hot local sensation at legs or ankles was 32 % and 25 % in DV-57-22-NS-
W10 and DV-57-22-NS-W0, respectively.
The local thermal sensation was not the main interest in the subjective assessment, thus it was rather
simpliﬁed. The subjects only indicated hot or cold sensation. Nevertheless, the votes included also warm
and cool local sensations, as it is presented in the instructions for participants in Appendix D.2. Figure 5.20
presents the percentage of votes for the local thermal sensation, both the hot and cold. The local thermal
sensation was important especially for the cases with BD and TD, when the clean air was supplied close to the
subjects above the table. The equivalent temperature of the thermal manikin indicated colder conditions for
head, back of neck, hands and forearms, as shown in Figure 5.15. The subjects reported hot local sensation on

5.3.3
SBS symptoms
The sensation of eye dryness was expected to occur rather with BD and TD, when the clean air was supplied
above the table, possibly with high air speed. However, Figure 5.21 shows that the subjects reported better
conditions regarding dry eyes under BD-57-22-NS-WT and BD-57-18,5-NS-WT, than during the other sessions.
In general, Figure 5.21 shows a substantial dispersion in votes comparing to the other surveyed SBS symptoms.
The mean of the votes of throat dryness was reported always above 80 %, where 100 % means no dry throat,
as shown in Figure I.4. Even higher and more narrow range of votes was collected regarding headache. In all
sessions, the mean of the votes for the severity of headache was above 85 %, where 100 % means no headache.
These results did not change even during the sessions with the highest exposure to the metabolic CO2 (in
sessions 2 and 8), as presented in Figure I.5.

Similar ﬁndings were reported in case of the other questions regarding well-being. In all sessions, the
mean votes from the questions related to ability to concentrate, drowsiness, performance and feeling well
were above 80 %, as shown in Figure I.6, I.7, I.9, and I.8 in Appendix I.1. The only exception was in session
MV-57-18-2-S-WT regarding drowsiness, where the mean of subjects’ answers was between 70 % and 90 %, as
presented in Figure I.7.

during the PM it was observed that the heated dummies provided more heat than the actual subjects. This fact
was found out by the increase in the room air temperature without changing any settings of the systems.

CHAPTER6
Discussion
Chapter 6 goes further through the presented results and their implication. First, the impact of the studied
parameters on the CO2 exposure is discussed. Diﬀerent air ﬂow interactions and their impact on CO2 concen-
tration distribution are described. The outcomes from the smoke visualizations are presented to support the
arguments. The results of the subjective perception of the environment are commented too. Moreover, the
present study and its results are further compared with the results of recently published study related to the
CO2 exposure. Finally, limitations of the present study are summarized.
6.1
Impact of the studied parameters on the CO2
concentration distribution
6.1.1
Impact on the exposure
The ﬁrst objective of this study was to examine whether and to what extent CO2 in the inhaled air diﬀers from
the CO2 concentration in the exhaust air. The results reporting this issue were presented in Figure 5.2. It is
clear that the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air diﬀers substantially from the CO2 concentration in the ex-
haust air. Moreover, the magnitude of the diﬀerence is aﬀected by the investigated parameters. For mixing air
distribution (MV) and ventilation with the box diﬀuser (BD) and the textile duct (TD) the CO2 concentration
in the inhaled air was higher than the exhaust air concentration (excluding session MV-68-18-2-S-WT with
higher ﬂow rate). These results imply that the CO2 bubble reported in the study of Ghahramani et al. [31]
exists and causes a higher concentration in the inhaled air. Ghahramani et al. reported that the initiated air
movement in the breathing zone by a small fan on the desk can dilute this bubble and substantially decrease in-
haled CO2 concentration. Although the use of a desk fan proved to be eﬃcient method to dilute the metabolic
CO2 in the breathing zone, there are still some disadvantages. The desk fan mixes the room air, which in the
presence of other room air pollutants might in fact deteriorate the inhaled air quality. Therefore, personalized
ventilation (PV), which supplies clean outdoor air to the breathing zone, might be a better solution than a desk
fan [8, 30, 50]. PV is advantageous in various ways. It can also use the room air and ﬁlter it before supplying
to the breathing zone. The air speed can be controlled individually, so every occupant can adjust the speed
according to his/her preferences.
Application of either the desk fan or the PV is an easy solution for spaces like a single or an open-space
oﬃce, or call-center, where occupants have their assigned work space and usually have their own desk. A
diﬀerent situation occurs in spaces like meeting rooms, where various number of occupants sit around one
table. In such cases, clean air supplied by a suitable diﬀuser above the table might be a solution. This option
was investigated in the present study by using BD and TD. The results did not show any decrease of the inhaled
CO2 concentration (Figure 5.2), although the use of the BD improved the PAQ of the subjects (Figure 5.16). This
issue is discussed later in this chapter. Further improvement of the box diﬀuser might lead to an enhancement
of its performance and decrease of the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air. The dimensions of the box diﬀuser
could be enlarged. The curtain-like pattern would be wider and reach the breathing zone of the occupants.
However, a further study should investigate this assumption with regard to thermal comfort of occupants and
the impact of supply air temperature on the performance of the box diﬀuser.
Although the supply ﬂow rate stayed the same for 9 out of 10 sessions, the CO2 concentration measured in
the exhaust air varied considerably. The ﬂow rate of 57 L/s was designed based on Equation 4.3. According to
==PAGE:100==
82
6 Discussion
this mass-balance equation, the excess concentration in the exhaust air should have been 600 ppm. The small
diﬀerences (up to 71 ppm in session BD-57-18,5-NS-WT) could be caused by variations in the generation rate
of metabolic CO2 of the occupants. The CO2 generation rate depends on several factors (e.g. metabolic rate,
or background CO2) and therefore it is not a constant value. However, in the sessions with displacement air
distribution the excess CO2 concentration in the exhaust air was substantially higher than 600 ppm. This was
due to the diﬀerent air distribution principle. With displacement air distribution method, the polluted air is
not mixed with the clean air, but it is stratiﬁed above a certain height and replaced by the clean air. Thus, the
CO2 concentration in the exhaust air is higher with the displacement air distribution.
On the contrary, the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air was substantially lower in DV-57-22-NS-W10
compared to the rest of the sessions. In this session, the displacement air distribution principle was working
without disturbance of the convective boundary layer. The resulting CO2 concentration in the inhaled air cor-
responded to the calculation presented in DS/EN 15251 [1]. The standard includes a formula corresponding
to the one used to calculate the designed supply ﬂow rate in the experiment (Equation 4.3). The formula in the
standard includes the impact of air distribution, i.e. ventilation eﬀectiveness. This method has already been
presented in chapter 1. The formula given in the standard is
Qh =
Gh
Ch,i −Ch,o
× 1
εv
,
(6.1)
where Qh is the ventilation rate required for health (L/s), Gh is the pollution load (g/s), Ch,i is the guideline
value of a pollution (g/L), Ch,e is the outdoor concentration of a pollution at air intake (g/L), and εv is the
ventilation eﬀectiveness [1]. The diﬀerence between the supply air temperature and the room air temperature
(at the height of 1,1 m) was -4 K in sessions with displacement air distribution (as implies from results in
Table 5.1). For this temperature diﬀerence, the ventilation eﬀectiveness was estimated to be 1,2 [2]. With
this adjustment the indoor CO2 concentration is calculated as in Equation 6.2. This value corresponds to the
obtained result of inhaled CO2 concentration presented in Figure 5.2.
Ci =
nocc × GCO2 × 1
εv
q × 3600 × 10−6
Ci =
8 × 15,5L/h × 1
1,2
57,4L/s × 3600 × 10−6 = 500ppm
(6.2)
In Equation 6.2, Ci is the CO2 concentration in exhaust (ppm), nocc is the number of occupants, GCO2 is the
CO2 generation by an occupant (L/h), εv is the ventilation eﬀectiveness, and q is the designed supply air ﬂow
(L/s).
The mass-balance equation of CO2 concentration is not a suitable calculation method for ventilation rates
for mixing air distribution under currently used assumptions. The assumption of complete mixing in the room
(i.e. equal CO2 concentrations across the occupied zone and in the exhaust air) and neglecting the existence
of higher CO2 concentrations in the inhaled air are incorrect. The supply ﬂow rate based on the mass-balance
equation was 57,4 L/s. With displacement air distribution this supply air ﬂow resulted in excess inhaled CO2
concentration smaller than 600 ppm, as it was estimated above in Equation 6.2. However, this result depended
on the occupants’ behaviour and the calm environment without air ﬂow disturbances (i.e. opening a window,
movement of the occupants), as discussed later in this section. The ventilation eﬀectiveness is diﬃcult to be
precisely estimated in the design process of ventilation systems, because it is aﬀected by numerous parameters
that are diﬃcult to predict. Some of those parameters are actual air ﬂow interaction in the room, behaviour
and activities of the occupants, furniture arrangement, and heat load strength and distribution. Moreover,
even in spaces with well-mixed air the CO2 bubble causes higher concentrations of inhaled CO2. The session
with MV-68-18-2-S-WT showed, that higher supply ﬂow rate did not eliminate the impact of the CO2 bubble
==PAGE:101==
6.1 Impact of the studied parameters on the CO2 concentration distribution
83
(Figure 5.2). However, the higher supply ﬂow rate decreased both the concentrations in the inhaled and in
the exhaust air. Therefore, a possible improvement regarding the CO2 exposure could be by lowering the
CO2 concentration limits prescribed by the standards. The desired CO2 concentration limit in this study was
1 000 ppm, assuming the same concentration in the exhaust and the inhaled air. This study showed that with
regular mixing air distribution methods, the inhaled CO2 concentration is higher than the required 1 000 ppm.
Therefore, the assumption of equal concentration in the exhaust and the inhaled air is incorrect. Instead, it
is recommended that the concentration limit for the indoor CO2 should be lower than the desired inhaled
CO2 concentration, when the mass-balance equation is used to deﬁne the supply ﬂow rate. In the current
study it was found that approximately 100 ppm lower CO2 concentration limit in the calculation is enough
so that the resulting inhaled CO2 concentration to be about 1 000 ppm. This leads to recommendation of
two separate values for CO2 concentration; one for the inhaled CO2 concentration, and another for the CO2
concentration in the exhaust air. Lowering the 1 000 ppm limit at the exhaust by 100 ppm would give the CO2
concentration above the outdoor level (Cc,i in Equation 4.3) equal to 500 ppm. This lower limit would lead to
a supply ﬂow rate of 68,8,L/s in this study (i.e. 8 occupants, generating 15,5 L/h of metabolic CO2). The ﬂow
rate is calculated as follows:
q = 8 ×
15,5L/h
3600 × 10−6 × 500ppm = 68,8L/s.
(6.3)
This means an increase in the supply ﬂow rate by 11,4 L/s compared to 57,4 L/s (Equation 4.4). The ﬂow
rate of 68,8 L/s would provide approximately 1 000 ppm in the inhaled air of the occupants. A similar ﬂow rate
was deﬁned by the calculation method for ventilation rate presented by DS/EN 15251 with the design criteria
for category B. The calculated ﬂow rate was 68,0 L/s and was used in session MV-68-18-2-S-WT. In fact, this
supply ﬂow rate resulted in an absolute inhaled CO2 concentration of 1 013 ppm and thus reaching the desired
limit. It is important to note, that the design criteria for category B in DS/EN 15251 are based on a diﬀerence
of 500 ppm between the outdoor air and the exhaust air concentration9. This supports the above mentioned
recommendation of lowering the recommended limit of 1 000 ppm by 100 ppm at the exhaust. Therefore, the
mass-balance equation for CO2 concentration might be a suitable calculation method for ventilation rates for
mixing air distribution, if certain correct assumptions are made. More research is required in order to gain a
better understanding of the relation between increasing supply ﬂow rate and decreasing diﬀerence between
the concentration in the inhaled and the exhaust air.
Impact of heat load distribution on the exposure
Heat load strength and distribution had a considerable impact not only on the inhaled CO2 concentration
but also on the CO2 concentration distribution throughout the room. This was a result of the air ﬂow interac-
tion, aﬀected by the buoyancy ﬂow generated by the solar heat load. Simultaneously, the heat load distribution
in the chamber was unequal, with the solar loads applied only on one side of the room. This hypothesis was
veriﬁed by the smoke visualizations. Figure 6.1 documents the smoke visualization run.
When the solar loads were simulated, both the warmer surface of the ﬂoor and that of the artiﬁcial window
heated up the air. This caused higher velocities on one side of the chamber. This air with higher velocity
was directed to the ceiling and therefore disturbing the air ﬂow from the diﬀusers. When no solar loads were
applied, the supply air ﬂow smoothly followed the ceiling and the wall surface. The disturbance of the supply
air ﬂow is noticeable in Figure 6.1a. In Figure 6.1a it can be seen that the disturbed air jets of the supply air
ﬂow were pushed away in the opposite direction from the artiﬁcial window and directed towards the lights.
The generated buoyancy ﬂow changed the air ﬂow interaction in the room, resulting in the better mixing. The
impact of the heat load strength and distribution was investigated in a CFD simulation as part of a recent
study [51]. The CFD model was based on the same climate chamber as the one used in the present study.
Although the model was rather simpliﬁed, the results commenting the impact of heat load distribution came
to the same conclusion. The modelled heat load from windows resulted in a better mixing of the clean air

supplied from ceiling slot diﬀusers with the polluted air in the room [51]. Figure 6.2 shows the results from
the CFD simulation. The vectors of the air speed (m/s) at the ceiling indicate the change of the air jets direction
when the solar loads were simulated.

Impact of number and location of diﬀusers on the exposure
The number of ceiling slot diﬀusers used to supply the clean air did not aﬀect the diﬀerence between
the inhaled CO2 concentration and the concentration in the exhaust air, as can be seen in Figure 5.2. This
indicates that the use of one or two slot diﬀusers in this case did not aﬀect the air ﬂow interaction substantially.
The diﬀerence between the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and in the exhaust air was 154 ppm in the
reference case with 2 slots, and 175 ppm in the session with 1 slot. However, this fractional impact could be
caused by the speciﬁc type of the diﬀuser, i.e. slot diﬀusers, and its ﬂow pattern. The ﬂow pattern of the slot
diﬀuser SLN 472 from Halton [37] was aligned to the ceiling surface. As mentioned above, it can be aﬀected
by buoyancy ﬂows generated by warm surfaces in the room. However, it does not interact considerably with
the thermal plumes generated by the occupants. The result might be diﬀerent with the use of another diﬀuser
type with the ﬂow patter directed down towards the occupants. Furthermore, a higher number of diﬀusers
decreases the supply ﬂow rate per diﬀuser. This leads to lower supply air velocities and thus results in a
diﬀerent air ﬂow interaction.
==PAGE:103==
6.1 Impact of the studied parameters on the CO2 concentration distribution
85
The box diﬀuser and the textile duct were located above the table. The intention was to decrease the inhaled
CO2 concentration by providing the clean air close to the breathing zone, before it is mixed with the polluted
air in the room. Nevertheless, the result did not meet the expectation. Both the BD and TD worked similar to
the mixing distribution principle. The air, although supplied above the table, mixed with the polluted exhaled
air before it reached the breathing zone of the occupants. Thus, the inhaled CO2 concentration was similar
to the ones obtained with ceiling slot diﬀusers. In case of the textile duct a diﬀerent installation height might
improve the CO2 exposure. For instance, a high impulse textile duct installed lower below the ceiling and with
nozzles directed towards the breathing zone of the occupants might decrease the inhaled CO2 concentration.
Or maybe even a low impulse textile duct installed lower below the ceiling could lead to an improvement of
the exposure. Nevertheless, the textile duct applied in this experiment was a semi-circular duct for ceiling
installations. The smoke visualization of the ﬂow pattern from the textile duct is shown in Figure 6.3. On the
ﬁgure, it can be seen that the supplied by the TD air did not reach the breathing zone of the occupants before
it mixed with the exhaled air. The box diﬀuser supplied the clean air even closer to the occupants, with its
bottom edge at the height of 2 m above the ﬂoor. The result obtained in BD-54-22-NS-WT was similar to the
reference case MV-57-18-2-S-WT. However, the design of the box diﬀuser could be further improved to reach
the desired eﬀect. The width of the box diﬀuser might have been small for this table and the arrangement of
the occupants. The clean air was supplied in a curtain-like pattern, given by the dimensions of the box diﬀuser.
Nevertheless, the curtain was narrow and did not reach the breathing zone of the occupants. This was veriﬁed
by the smoke visualization, as shown in Figure 6.4a.
Figure 6.3: The smoke visualization for session TD-57-22-NS-WT. The clean air was supplied above the table
Impact of distance between occupants on the exposure
The frontal distance between occupants was shorten by 15 cm from 110 cm to 95 cm when using the narrow
table. By exchanging the table, the arrangement around the table changed as well. None of those changes had
an impact on the inhaled CO2 concentration. In a space with mixing air distribution the proximity of the
occupants with frontal distance larger than 95 cm the elevated inhaled CO2 concentration might be caused
by the metabolic CO2 generated by the occupant himself/herself. Although various studies focused on a
cross-infection and distance between occupants were done [52], further research is needed to gain a better
understanding of this issue.
Impact of occupants’ behaviour on the exposure
As mentioned earlier in this chapter, the lower inhaled CO2 concentration obtained in session DV-57-22-
NS-W10 was highly conditioned by the occupants’ behavior. The occupants were sitting still, without any
considerable movements. Occasionally, they were leaning forward or backward, according to their activity
(e.g. reading, writing, using an electrical device). The subjects’ abdomens were 10 cm away from the edge of
the table. Overall, there was a calm environment in the chamber with displacement ventilation. In such an
==PAGE:104==
86
6 Discussion
environment, the convective boundary layer (CBL) easily develops and without any disturbance it brings the
clean air up to the breathing zone. However, the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air increased substantially,
once the CBL was interrupted by closing the gap between the occupant’s torso and the table. This result is
shown in Figure 5.2. Although 0 cm distance of the body from the table increases exposure to metabolic CO2,
studies have shown that it can have also positive eﬀect on the exposure to pollutants from the ﬂoor or from
the body [30]. Moreover, if occupants move freely (e.g. standing up, walking around, or going to a printing
room), or any other distraction to the calm environment occurs (e.g. opening a window or a door), the CBL
may be disturbed or not even developed. As a result the inhaled CO2 concentration may increase. However,
displacement ventilation can be supported by personalized ventilation (PV). PV can supply clean air to the
breathing zone with a constant air velocity that is not aﬀected by the above mentioned factors. PV can be
either a movable panel located on the desk [8], or incorporated in the desk and supplying clean air through a
slot close to the occupant’s abdomen [50].
Impact of supply air temperature on the exposure
The importance of an appropriate design of the air distribution was supported by the results obtained in
session BD-57-18,5-NS-WT. The supply air temperature was lowered by 3,5 °C compared to 22 °C in session
BD-57-22-NS-WT. The supply of colder air did not improve the CO2 exposure. On the contrary, the inhaled
CO2 concentration increased substantially. It was the highest measured CO2 concentration in the inhaled air
among all sessions, as shown in Figure 5.2. This could be a result of diﬀerent air ﬂow interaction, caused by
the colder and heavier air supplied above the table. The cold air was falling towards the table faster than the
warmer supply air (in BD-57-22-NS-WT) and then was spreading out further into the sides approximately at
the table height. This is supported by the fact that the measured air speed at the height of 1,1 m behind the
manikin was almost double than in the other three measured heights This can be seen in Figure 5.12b. The
air velocity of the clean air spreaded to the sides was probably high enough to prevent the clean air to be
entrained by the free convective ﬂow around the occupant’s body. Moreover, the box diﬀuser was not wide
enough for the speciﬁc arrangement of the occupants around the table. The curtain-like ﬂow of the clean air
was too narrow and did not reach the breathing zone of the occupants. This fact was intensiﬁed by lowering
the supply air temperature, as shown in Figure 6.4a. When the supply air was 22 °C, the ﬂow was wider due
to the lower air speed. This can be seen in Figure 6.4b.
(a) BD with tsup of 18,5 °C
(b) BD with tsup of 22 °C
Figure 6.4: Smoke visualization with BD and diﬀerent supply air temperatures tsup during the sessions BD-57-
22-NS-WT and BD-57-18,5-NS-WT
6.1.2
Representative CO2 measuring points in the room
The CO2 concentration was measured above the table at the heights of 0,5 m and 1,0 m. The measuring point at
0,5 m above the table was at the same level as the subjects’ heads. It can be seen in Figure 4.24 that the sampling
tube was attached to a small stand on the table, ending at the height of 0,5 m above the desk. These measuring
points were monitoring the CO2 concentration close to the breathing zone of the occupants. Moreover, the
measurements aimed to indicate a possible suitable location for a CO2 sensor in practice. For mixing air
distribution with the ceiling slot diﬀusers, the concentration measured in 1,0 m height above the table was
==PAGE:105==
6.1 Impact of the studied parameters on the CO2 concentration distribution
87
closer, than the concentration in the 0,5 m, to the inhaled CO2 concentration. On the contrary, the results
were diﬀerent for displacement air distribution and also for the ventilation with the box diﬀuser and the
textile. The CO2 concentration measured at 0,5 m above the table was ﬂuctuating substantially in most of the
sessions. The reason of the ﬂuctuations in sessions with BD was that at this distance the ﬂow supplied from
the BD probably developed and the turbulence intensity increased promoting mixing with the surrounding
air with high CO2 concentration. Additionally, the placement of the measuring point was relatively close to
the occupants’ exhaled air. Most of the time in the chamber, the subjects were reading, writing, or using their
smart phones. This behaviour resulted in the exhalation directed towards the desk. Nevertheless, occasionally
the subjects looked up and then exhaled directly towards the sampling tube at 0,5 m above the table. That
was one of the reasons for the high uncertainties (up to 11,4 % uncertainty), mentioned in section 5.1.5. An
example of the CO2 concentration measured in time is shown in Figure 6.5.
Figure 6.5: Absolute CO2 concentration in time measured above the table at 0,5 m and 1,0 m and in the exhaust
air during the session MV-57-18-2-S-NT
The measuring points above the table were monitored by INNOVA 2, which was the measuring instrument
with the longest sampling interval, as shown in Table 4.4. The total number of samples taken during one
session was approximately 42. This is another reason for the higher measurement uncertainties of the mean
CO2 concentrations at these measuring points. The large ﬂuctuations indicated that any location close to
the occupants and their exhalation might not be suitable for the placement of a CO2 sensor. The CO2-based
control would be negatively aﬀected by the ﬂuctuations. However, this might be compensated by using a
ﬂoating average (moving or running average) for the controlling value. A ﬂoating average is a calculation
that analyzes data points by creating a series of averages of diﬀerent subsets of the full data set [53]. One
average can be based on values measured during a chosen time duration, e.g. 5 min. The ﬁrst element of the
ﬂoating average is deﬁned by taking the average of the initial subset (e.g. values measured during the ﬁrst
5 min). Then the subset is modiﬁed by shifting forward, when the ﬁrst number is excluded, and the next value
is included in the subset. This is a common way to treat time series data in order to smooth out short-term
ﬂuctuations and highlight longer-term trends or cycles [53]. This could be a more suitable control strategy with
the sensor placed closer to the occupants. Table 6.1 presents the mean absolute CO2 concentrations measured
above the table with diﬀerent data treatments. The "normal" mean CO2 concentration was calculated based
on single measured concentrations. The "ﬂoating average" mean CO2 concentration was calculated based on
ﬂoating averages with the time subset of 5 min. The "ﬂoating average" mean CO2 concentrations are almost
identical to the "normal" mean CO2 concentrations, however the standard deviations are smaller in case of the
"ﬂoating average" concentrations. Furthermore, it is important to mention that a 5 min subset included only
two measured concentrations due to the long sampling interval of INNOVA 2. Extending the time of ﬂoating
average to 10 min would lead to three measured concentrations in a subset. This might still not be enough to
==PAGE:106==
88
6 Discussion
obtain a reliable value. Therefore, a measuring instrument with a higher sampling rate would provide more
results and thus lead to a more reliable value. The time constant of the system must be also taken into account
when designing the control of the ventilation system.

The location of the highest CO2 concentration diﬀered for diﬀerent air distribution methods. With dis-
placement air distribution the highest CO2 concentration was measured at the north wall at 1,8 m height
(when excluding the concentrations measured above the table). This was the furthest measuring point from
the displacement diﬀuser. Displacement ventilation works on the principle of letting warm contaminants rise
to the ceiling, where being extracted, and supplying cool clean air at the ﬂoor level. The clean air is heated
up by heat sources and rises upwards, being replaced by constantly supplied air [42]. Therefore, the highest
concentrations were observed in the upper part of the chamber and even higher closer to the exhaust valve.
On the contrary, with BD and TD the highest CO2 concentration was measured at 1,1 m height behind the
thermal manikin (excluding the concentrations measured above the table). This can be explained by the air
ﬂow interaction caused by the ﬂow patterns of these supply diﬀusers. When the clean air was supplied above
the table, close to the breathing zone of the occupants, the supply air was mixing with the exhaled air in this
zone and by its current forced out to the rest of the occupied zone. Due to the ﬂow pattern, the exhaled air was
probably mixed with the clean air, pushed down towards the table, and then spread out in the height of the
table. This idea was supported by the fact that the concentration measured at 0,5 m above the table increased
considerably in the sessions with BD. The ﬁrst measuring point that this dislodged air reached after hitting
the table was the measuring point at 1,1 m behind the thermal manikin. This hypothesis could be supported
by measuring in additional points on a stand located behind subject 4 (the location of the subject can be seen
in Figure 4.9), i.e. symmetrically on the other side of the table.
The CO2 concentration was also measured at three heights on three of the walls in the chamber (exclud-
ing the wall with the artiﬁcial window). In case of complete mixing, the concentration should be relatively
equal at each measured point. Nevertheless, the results did not support this assumption. With mixing air
distribution the CO2 concentration at the three walls varied in ranges 163 ppm, 201 ppm, 158 ppm, 115 ppm,
and 142 ppm within the sessions 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5, respectively (speciﬁcation of the sessions is presented in
Table 4.2). The ranges were fractionally smaller at each wall separately, although often over 100 ppm. Similar
ranges of the measured CO2 concentration at the walls were obtained with BD and TD. Speciﬁcally, the ranges
were 138 ppm, 162 ppm, and 162 ppm for sessions 8, 9, and 10, respectively. Substantially larger ranges were
expected and also measured with displacement air distribution (250 ppm and 305 ppm for session 6 and 7,
respectively). The expected concentration stratiﬁcation in the conditions with DV increased proportionally
==PAGE:107==
6.1 Impact of the studied parameters on the CO2 concentration distribution
89
with height only at the north wall. A generally calm environment in the chamber was expected within the
displacement air distribution.
One of the reasons to measure the concentration at the walls was to identify a suitable location for place-
ment of a CO2 sensor. In section 1.3 the current recommendations in the standards for a CO2 sensor place-
ment were introduced. The recommendations advise to place the sensor at a mid-height in a room, away from
doorways, radiators, windows, and air inlets. The mean CO2 concentrations in the measured heights were pre-
sented in Table 5.4. The results of this study showed, that the average measured CO2 concentration at the walls
with mixing air distribution methods, including the box diﬀuser and the textile duct, were closer to the exhaust
air concentration than to the concentrations measured in the inhaled air. Despite the fact that the measured
ranges were considerable. This ﬁnding is presented in Table 6.2. Due to no regular pattern among the mea-
suring heights, it is diﬃcult to deﬁne one suitable height for sensor placement. Moreover, the concentration
measured in the exhaust air was closer to the inhaled CO2 concentration than the average CO2 concentrations
measured at the walls. Therefore, the recommended sensor placement for mixing air distribution methods is
in the exhaust air. This recommendation is valid only if the CO2 sensor controls only one ventilated room. As
already discussed in the beginning of this section, the concentration in the exhaust air is substantially lower
than the CO2 exposure of occupants. To provide lower inhaled CO2 concentration, the control set point mon-
itored by the CO2 sensor in the exhaust should be lowered by 100 ppm - 200 ppm. This applies for all indoor
concentration limits listed in Table 1.2. For example, category C in DS/EN 15251 requires the indoor CO2
concentration to be 800 ppm above the outdoor level. In order to obtain approximately this concentration in
the inhaled air, the control system should operate with a set point of 700 ppm above the outdoor level. It is
not easy to provide a recommendation for a CO2 placement for displacement air distribution. In case that
displacement air distribution works as its principle assumes, the inhaled CO2 concentration is substantially
lower than at any other point in the room, as shown in Figure 5.7 and 5.8. The closest CO2 concentration was
measured at the height of 0,6 m behind subject 6 (Figure 5.5). But as it was discussed earlier in this section,
the success of the displacement air distribution principle is dependent on many factors that are diﬃcult to
restrict.
However, the observed CO2 concentration at the walls were not regular. The heat load distribution, the
wall temperature, and the ﬂow direction and interaction might have had an impact on the measured CO2 con-
centration. It should be further visualized and studied. The present study showed that the CO2 concentration
measured at the walls is very much sensitive to the above mentioned factors. Therefore, it might not be a
reliable to place a CO2 sensor on a wall.

6 Discussion
6.1.3
CO2 as an adequate indicator of other indoor air pollutants
The additional sessions performed in this study aimed to assess the CO2 as a proper indicator of other indoor
pollutants. However, this was not the main objective of this study and therefore the approach was simpli-
ﬁed. The results were presented in Figure 5.10. Extensive studies were conducted in order to identify CO2
as an adequate indicator for indoor air pollution [54, 55]. Ramalho et al. [54] investigated the association of
CO2 with other indoor air pollutants. Their study was based on an extensive survey in 567 dwellings and
310 educational premises in France. They focused on the correlation between indoor CO2 concentration and
among others formaldehyde, benzene, PM2,5 or PM10. The results of their analysis showed a statistically signif-
icant correlation between the concentration of CO2 and formaldehyde and benzene in both surveys. However,
the strength of the correlations was weak. Also the season seemed to have a major role. The correlations
were stronger during the heating season, compared to the non-heating season [54]. Nevertheless, the authors
concluded that the CO2 concentration cannot be considered as a unique indicator of indoor air quality. Simi-
lar conclusions were made by Chatzidiakou [55], mentioning the inadequacy of the current regulatory model
relying on CO2 levels to provide healthy and comfortable environment in schools.
6.2
Subjective assessment of the environment by the
occupants
The results of the subjective assessment were used to provide a basic evaluation with an estimation of possible
trends. The results presented in section 5.3 showed a relatively large dispersion in the answers of the subjects.
Larger number of subjects would be necessary to obtain a substantial sample for a statistical analysis.
The interesting ﬁnding in the subjective assessment was the perception of the air quality with regard to
the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air. As mentioned in section 5.3.1, the biggest diﬀerence in the absolute
inhaled concentration between the sessions was not projected in the PAQ of the subjects. The absolute inhaled
CO2 concentration was 405 ppm higher in session BD-57-18,5-NS-WT compared to DV-57-22-NS-W10, but
the PAQ was better in the case of BD than with DV. These results are presented in Figure 5.1. The results of the
PAQ are shown in Figure 5.16. The diﬀerences between these two sessions were the air distribution methods,
the location of the diﬀuser, and the air velocity close to the occupants. In the case with the box diﬀuser, the
cool clean air was supplied above the table and was causing air movement close to the occupants’ breathing
zone. On the contrary, the displacement air distribution is characterized by lower air velocities in general (Fig-
ure 5.12b). This could substantially aﬀect the perception of the air quality. Melikov et al.[7] reported that the
facial air movement improved the acceptability of the inhaled air compare to the conditions without the air
movement. Similar results were presented by Dalewski et al. [8] when using personalized ventilation. With
displacement air distribution, the PAQ increases with increasing supply ﬂow rate. On the contrary, the thermal
comfort within displacement air distribution increases when the clean air is supplied with lower temperature
and lower supply ﬂow rate. Since most of the inhaled air within displacement air distribution is from the con-
vective boundary layer which is warm, and therefore aﬀects negatively the PAQ. The use of PV signiﬁcantly
improved the PAQ [8]. Furthermore, Figure 5.16 shows fractionally better PAQ with the box diﬀuser and tsup
equal to 18,5 °C than with higher tsup equal to 22 °C. This corresponds to ﬁndings of Fang et al. [9] reporting
that temperature has a strong impact on PAQ. The PAQ increases with decreasing air temperature and humid-
ity. The odour perception, presented in Figure I.1, was very low with narrow dispersion of the votes in session
BD-57-18,5-NS-WT. The votes were in smaller range than in BD-57-22-NS-WT with higher tsup. Although the
diﬀerence is fractional and could have been random, it corresponds with the results of Feng’s study [9].
6.3
Comparison with another study related to exposure to
metabolic CO2
Ghahramani et al. [31] published results from a study focused on CO2 exposure of occupants. The study
compared the CO2 concentration measured in the breathing zone with the measurements by a wearable sensor
==PAGE:109==
6.3 Comparison with another study related to exposure to metabolic CO2
91
placed on the chest of the subjects. Like the present thesis, the study assumed and investigated an increased
exposure to CO2 of the occupants compared to background CO2 concentration. The wearable sensor was
studied as a possible appropriate monitoring device that would provide results corresponding to the actual
inhaled CO2 concentration.
Compared to the presented study, the experiment comprised only human subjects. Therefore, the CO2
concentration was not measured directly in the inhaled air, but very close to the nostrils of the subjects. This
concentration was deﬁned as the inhalation zone CO2 concentration. The inhalation zone CO2 concentration
was measured by a gas analyzer, that was taking sample every 1 s [31]. The wearable sensor was worn around
the neck of the subjects, placed on the chest. The background CO2 concentration was monitored as well, with
samples taken every 5 s. However, the location of the sensor measuring the background concentration was not
speciﬁed in the published results. The air change rate was 4 hr-1 during the entire experiment. The impact of
a diﬀerent air distribution was of no interest in the study, as the clean air was supplied only by the mixing air
distribution. The clean air was supplied by a single 4-way ceiling diﬀuser and the exhaust air was discharged
through a single ceiling exhaust grille. In the study it is not further speciﬁed, whether other heat sources
(e.g. solar gains) were present or simulated in the chamber. This in fact might have a substantial impact on
the CO2 exposure of the occupants. The present study showed that diﬀerent heat load distribution aﬀected
the ﬂow interaction in the room and consequently the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air. Nevertheless,
Ghahramani et al. aimed at investigating other parameters. The examined parameters were diﬀerent breathing
activities under diﬀerent desk settings. The study did not investigate the impact of parameters like heat load
distribution, a diﬀuser type and location, or diﬀerent supply air ﬂow rates and temperatures. Devices that
generate additional heat close to the occupant may aﬀect the ﬂow interaction. Moreover, the objects on the
desk were obstacles in front of the occupant, possibly having an impact on the investigated CO2 exposure. The
result of the study showed that there was no considerable diﬀerence in the inhalation zone CO2 concentration
between the settings with the empty desk, the laptop, and the monitor.
Every subject performed each of the conditions for 5 minutes. Nevertheless, this short time period might
be insuﬃcient to obtain reliable results. Kierat et al. (2018) studied the accurate assessment of exposure by
"fast" and "slow" gas concentration meter [56]. The "fast" concentration meter had a sampling rate of 4 Hz. The
study suggested that the measuring time of approximately 15 min is required to obtain the mean concentration
values with 5 % uncertainty. Unfortunately, Ghahramani et al. did not provide more information about the
procedure of their experiment. It was not speciﬁed, whether there was always only one subject in the chamber
at a time or not. Similarly, it did not describe the procedure in time. Thus, the time between entering the
chamber and beginning of measuring is unknown. This raises a doubt, whether the obtained results are reli-
able. After a subject enters the chamber, the CO2 concentration in the room starts to increase, and this build
up is reﬂected in the CO2 exposure as well. This has been observed in the present study and it is shown in Fig-
ure 6.6. Moreover, the measured background CO2 concentration in the reviewed study was 650 ± 100 ppm [31].
Although the usual CO2 concentration in outdoor air is 400 ± 50 ppm, a reason for the higher background CO2
concentration was not further explained in the paper.
The use of the breathing manikin in the present study allowed to measure the CO2 concentration directly
in the inhaled air. It also enabled to deﬁne a breathing mode. As mentioned in section 4.4.6, the breath-
ing mode of the manikin in this study was inhalation through the mouth and exhalation through the nose.
Ghahramani et al. did not specify, whether the subjects were asked to breath in any certain way. Neverthe-
less, they mentioned the eﬀect of a diﬀerent way of exhalation when explaining some of the obtained results.
This information is also important regarding the inhalation zone CO2 concentration measurements that were
performed approximately 1 cm from the nostrils. Bivolarova et al. [30] reported that the exhalation from the
nose and inhalation from the mouth increased the exposure to the pollution generated closer to the breathing
zone compared to the case with inhalation from the nose and exhalation from the mouth. This shows that the
breathing mode has an impact on the exposure and should be considered in the investigation of the occupants’
exposure.
The data analysis in the reviewed study was based on the mean and standard deviations of the measured
CO2 concentrations. However, this approach is questionable. The mean and the standard deviation were
==PAGE:110==
92
6 Discussion
Figure 6.6: Absolute CO2 concentration in time measured in the exhaust and in the inhaled air (exposure
concentration) - MV-57-18-2-S-WT. These results were obtained with 7 subjects and one breathing thermal
manikin exhaling CO2
deﬁned for each condition from the entire period of 5 min. The sensors were placed close to the nostrils and
on the chest of the subject, thus the data include the CO2 concentration measured during both the inhalation
and exhalation period. The results of Kierat et al. [56] showed that the assessment of the exposure can be
incorrect when the tracer gas concentration is estimated based on the concentration measurements during
the entire breathing cycle. The assessment should be done based on the concentration measured during the
inhalation period only. In the present study, the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air was measured by a "slow"
measuring device, namely INNOVA 5. As shown in Table 4.4, the sampling time for INNOVA 5 was 1,75 min
and the analyzing time of one sample was 35 s. Therefore, the inhaled air from the manikin’s mouth was led
into the jar where the CO2 concentration was measured.
The results of the study by Ghahramani et al. [31] showed that the lowest inhalation zone CO2 concen-
tration was obtained with a rotating fan placed on the desk. This indicated that the initiated air movement
(approx. 0,5 m/s) was enough to burst the CO2 bubble to some extent. The activity did not cause such a con-
siderable diﬀerence, although the inhalation zone CO2 concentration was the lowest for the "looking-straight"
activity. This was justiﬁed by the exhaled air moving straight from the mouth in a jet like fashion [31]. Never-
theless, all studied parameters (desk settings and activities) had a signiﬁcant impact on the resulting concen-
tration. In general, the wearable sensor measured lower CO2 concentrations than the ones in the inhalation
zone. The measured CO2 concentration was 300 ppm lower compared to the inhalation zone CO2 concen-
tration measured with the desk with the rotating fan condition. The CO2 concentration measured by the
wearable sensor was lower by up to 600 ppm compared to the inhalation zone CO2 concentration for the other
three desk setting scenarios. In 82,7 % of the cases the wearable sensor measured lower CO2 concentration
than the sensors placed close to the nostrils [31]. Except the condition with the desk fan, the inhalation zone
CO2 concentration was approximately 70 % higher than the CO2 concentration measured on the chest by the
wearable sensor. Also in the present study the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air was higher than in the
exhaust air, as already reported. With mixing air distribution the inhaled CO2 concentration was in average
30 % higher than in the exhaust air. Similarly, with mixing air distribution the inhaled CO2 concentration
was in average 30 % higher than the CO2 concentration measured at the walls (at the heights of 1,1 m, 1,4 m,
and 1,8 m). However, the preferable location for a CO2 sensor placement might be in the exhaust duct. The
==PAGE:111==
6.4 Limitations of the present study
93
CO2 concentration diﬀers a lot along the walls and it is very sensitive to the factors like air ﬂow interaction,
wall temperature, or heat load distribution. Nevertheless, with a CO2 sensor located in the exhaust duct the
diﬀerence between the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and the exhaust air should be taken into account.
Ghrahramani et al. plan to focus on more suitable ways to measure the CO2 exposure. Similarly, the further
research should be focused on optimizing the fan and air movement settings for minimizing the CO2 concen-
tration in the breathing zone, while maintaining high energy eﬃciency and personal thermal comfort [31].
To conclude, the measurements by the sensor mounted around the neck were found to be not a gener-
alizable solution. The CO2 bubble exists under regular ventilation systems, that do not provide higher air
velocity in the breathing zone. This fact has been disproved in the present study, where the displacement air
distribution provided clean air to the breathing zone, resulting in a low CO2 concentration in the inhaled air.
However, this result is conditioned by the occupants’ behaviour when disturbing the CBL by obstacles, or by
body movements or activity diminishes the eﬀect of displacement air distribution.
6.4
Limitations of the present study
The presented study was limited by its extent and the available time duration. Therefore, the number of per-
formed sessions was restricted to ten. As a result, the impact of numerous interesting parameters on inhaled
CO2 concentration and its distribution in the room were not investigated. Many of the sessions were based on
mixing air distribution as the most common type of air distribution in current practice. One of the excluded
parameters was the rotated position of the table with its axis parallel to the artiﬁcial window, which would
lead to diﬀerent interaction of the buoyancy ﬂows and the ventilation ﬂow in the room. In this case, diﬀerent
orientation of the box diﬀuser and the textile duct could be considered. The next parameter excluded from the
study was the location of the breathing manikin around the table. However, including this parameter would
substantially increase the number of sessions, even if performed only under the reference case conditions. The
impact of the supply ﬂow rate was investigated only with the mixing air distribution and only with two supply
ﬂow rates. The impact of a higher ﬂow rate would most likely change the performance of the other air distri-
bution methods applied in this study as well. The potential of non-uniform air distribution for reduction of
exposure to CO2, i.e. air supply at the location of the occupants, could be studied in deep with BD and TD at el-
evated ventilation rate The impact of diﬀerent locations of the displacement diﬀuser or a further improvement
of the box diﬀuser prototype are other parameters that needs to be studied as well. Finally, the experiment
was conducted only under simulated summer conditions. Winter conditions include among others diﬀerent
ambient room air temperature, local heat sources, and diﬀerent supply air temperatures. All these param-
eters would probably result in a diﬀerent air ﬂow interaction and consequently also diﬀerent concentration
distribution in the room.
The number of measuring points of the CO2 concentration was limited by the number of available mea-
suring instruments. More sensors or sampling channels would enable an observation at more points. Thus,
a better picture of the CO2 concentration distribution in the room could be obtained. There were two stands
in the chamber, with dissimilar heights of the measuring points. More sensors would allow measuring at more
locations in the chamber at more heights. For instance, measuring closer to the artiﬁcial window might help
deﬁning the air ﬂow interaction and the consequent CO2 dispersion. Similarly, the concentration could be
measured at additional points at the walls, horizontally as well as vertically. Moreover, measuring equipment
with shorter sampling time would enable to obtain more samples. This could lead to smaller uncertainties of
the measured data, or it could shorten the duration of the sessions.
Smaller distances between the occupants than the ones studied here (110 cm and 95 cm) should be fur-
ther investigated to observe any possible relation between the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and the
changing frontal distance.
The validation of CO2 as an adequate indicator of other pollutants was a supplemental investigation of the
study. The investigation was hence performed only as the additional sessions with a rather simpliﬁed approach.
The pollutants could be simulated by tracer gas. However, for ethical reasons, the additional pollution was
==PAGE:112==
94
6 Discussion
simulated by dosing extra CO2 in the background. This decreased reliability of the results. Results with dosing
another type of tracer gas might be more representing. As has been noted, this issue was not the main objective
of this study and should be considered as a subject matter for further research.
The number of subjects was insuﬃcient to perform reliable statistical analysis of the results of the subjec-
tive questionnaires. The correlation between diﬀerent CO2 exposure levels, the supply air temperature, the
thermal comfort, and the perceived air quality should be a subject matter of a future research.
==PAGE:113==
CHAPTER7
Conclusion
The pivotal focus of this study was on the assessment of a realistic CO2 exposure under various conditions
in a room. The factors investigated in the study were supply air distributions, heat load distribution in the
room, number and location of diﬀusers, and the occupants’ behaviour. The CO2 concentration was monitored
at numerous points in the chamber in order to identify its distribution. The thermal environment during the
sessions was observed as well. Moreover, the subjective questionnaires facilitated the comparison of objective
and subjective assessment of the indoor environment. Additionally to the measurements during the sessions
with subjects, physical measurements were performed at simulated room occupancy to investigate the air
temperature, the mean air speed, and the draft rating in the occupied zone in the chamber.
The most important ﬁndings are summarized in the following:
• There was a substantial diﬀerence in the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and the exhaust air from
the chamber (the CO2 was up to 347 ppm (66 %) higher than the concentration in the exhaust air);
• For the sessions with mixing air distribution, the excess CO2 concentration in the inhaled air was in
average 30 % higher than in the exhaust;
• The air distribution method was of a high importance, when aiming at CO2 levels lower than 1 000 ppm
in the inhaled air;
• Using the mass-balance equation to calculate the ventilation rate, needed to achieve a maximum CO2
concentration of 1 000 ppm and assuming well-mixed environment ventilated with mixing air distribu-
tion, may lead to an incorrect estimation of the personal exposure to CO2;
• The heat load distribution had a great impact on the diﬀerence between the CO2 concentration in the
inhaled air and the exhaust air. The buoyancy ﬂow generated by the solar heat load changed the airﬂow
interaction in the room and led to a decrease of the CO2 in the inhaled air and to an increase of uniformity
of the CO2 distribution in the room;
• The number and the position of the used ceiling slot diﬀusers did not have any substantial impact on the
CO2 concentration in the inhaled air under the studied conditions (location of table and heat sources,
supply ﬂow rates, etc.);
• The supply of clean air from the box diﬀuser and the textile duct located closer to the breathing zone did
not improve the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air, comparing to the mixing air distribution with the
ceiling diﬀusers. Moreover, providing the clean air above the table did not decreased the CO2 measured
0,5 m above the table;
• Decreasing the temperature of the air supplied by the box diﬀuser above the table increased the CO2
concentration in the inhaled air;
• The reduction of the frontal distance between the occupants from 110 cm to 95 cm did not cause any
noticeable increase of the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air;
• The displacement ventilation provided the lowest CO2 concentration in the inhaled air, but this fact was
highly conditioned by the occupants’ behaviour. With the 10 cm distance between the torso and the edge
of the table, the excess mean CO2 concentration in the inhaled air was 291 ppm (36 %) lower than in the
exhaust air. Closing this gap to 0 cm by occupant sitting closer to the table decreased the excess mean
CO2 concentration to 40 ppm (5 %);
==PAGE:114==
96
7 Conclusion
• The CO2 concentration measured above the table varied substantially, depending on the occupants’ be-
haviour (a diﬀerent activity corresponded to a diﬀerent direction of exhaled air, i.e. reading, looking
straight, etc.) and their actual distance from the sensor;
• The mean CO2 concentration above the table was closer to the CO2 concentration in the inhaled air in
the case with mixing ventilation. For displacement air distribution and the ventilation with both the box
diﬀuser and the textile duct, the mean CO2 concentration above the table in both measuring heights was
generally closer to the exhaust CO2 concentration;
• Non-uniformity of the CO2 concentration was found in the room during all studied air distribution
methods. For mixing air distribution, the non-uniformity in the CO2 concentration measured at all
measuring points (excluding inhaled air and supply concentration) was in range of up to 586 ppm. For
ventilation with the box diﬀuser and the textile duct, the range was up to 384 ppm. A non-uniform
CO2 concentration distribution was expected for displacement ventilation. The CO2 concentration non-
uniformity was up to 480 ppm;
• The location with the highest mean CO2 concentration measured in the occupied zone depended on the
air distribution, and the diﬀuser location. The highest mean CO2 concentration with mixing ventilation
was at 30 cm behind the thermal manikin and 1,7 m height, for the displacement ventilation at 20 cm
behind subject 6 and 1,4 m height, and with the box diﬀuser and the textile duct at 30 cm behind the
thermal manikin and 1,1 m height;
• The CO2 concentration measured at the walls diﬀered and ranged between 797 ppm and 1 312 ppm
during all sessions;
• The absolute concentration measured at the walls was closer to the level measured in the exhaust air
than in the inhaled air. The concentration measured in the exhaust air was closer to the concentration in
the inhaled air than the average concentration at the walls;
• Measurement of CO2 concentration was not proved to be an appropriate indicator of exposure to other
pollutants in the indoor air;
• The substantial diﬀerences in the inhaled air concentration between the studied air distribution methods
were not reﬂected in the subjective assessment of PAQ;
• In all sessions, PAQ was over 80 % after one hour of the subjects’ entry to the chamber.
Recommendations for further studies:
• More variations of the heat load strength and distribution, its impact on the ﬂow interaction, and the
impact on the CO2 exposure should be investigated;
• Type and various locations of a diﬀuser should be studied, considering diﬀerent air distribution methods;
• The box diﬀuser prototype should be optimized in order to improve its performance with the focus on
providing clean air to the breathing zone of occupants;
• The impact of diﬀerent supply air ﬂow rates in various air distribution methods should be studied. The
results might help to assess diﬀerent calculation methods of the ventilation rate;
• The impact of other occupants’ presence on the CO2 exposure should be investigated;
• The correlation between a higher CO2 concentration in the inhaled air and the PAQ of the occupants
should be further investigated with regard to the bias by thermal comfort and perceived freshness.
==PAGE:115==
7 Conclusion
97
Recommendations for updating of standards:
• The requirements in the present standards should be updated considering the diﬀerence between the
CO2 concentration in inhaled air and at the exhaust as well as the air distribution principle;
• A suitable location for a CO2 sensor with mixing air distribution is in the exhaust duct (in case the sensor
controls ventilation of only the monitored room). The limit in such a control system should be lowered
by at least 100 ppm in order to obtain an inhaled CO2 concentration below the desired limit;
• A suitable location for a CO2 sensor with displacement air distribution is at the wall at the height of
1,8 m or in the exhaust duct (in case the sensor controls ventilation of only the monitored room). The
limit in such a control system should be lowered by at least 100 ppm in order to obtain an inhaled CO2
concentration below the desired limit.

A Facilities and measuring equipment
107
Air transporting system of the artiﬁcial lungs
The air system and the tracer gas dosing system are placed in the artiﬁcial lungs box, as shown in Fig-
ure 4.19. The humidifying system and system for controlling the exhaled air temperature are located outside
of the box. The inhalation and exhalation are generated in 2 separate air circuits. Each circuit has an air pump
(Figure A.9, position 1 and 4) and a solenoid valve (Figure A.9, position 2 and 5). The solenoid valves are con-
trolled by timers (position 9), that direct the air according to the actual breathing mode. The exhalation pump
continuously pumps the clean air into the solenoid valve. During the 2,5 s of exhalation, the valve leads the
air through to the thermal manikin to be exhaled, after dosing of the tracer gas (position 6) and temperature
adjustment (position 11). During the rest of the cycle, i.e. for 3,5 s, the clean air is released out through the
solenoid valve. A tracer gas, CO2 in this experiment, is dosed continuously with a given ﬂow rate. During
exhalation period, the timers position the solenoid valve (position 7) so the tracer gas is mixed with the clean
air in exhalation circuit. In the rest of the breathing cycle, the tracer gas is released out from the artiﬁcial
lungs’ system. CO2 was dosed into the exhalation circuit through a gas rotameter, with the designed ﬂow rate
of 0,77 L/ min. The inhalation pump (position 1) also works continuously and during the inhalation phase it
takes the air from the thermal manikin. During the exhalation and the break, the solenoid valve (position 2)
closes the inlet from the manikin and circulates the air with a by-pass. The placement of tubes for the clean
air (for exhalation of the manikin) and release of the air inhaled by the manikin must be chosen correctly, so
that no contaminated air is mixed with the clean air sucked by the artiﬁcial lungs.
Figure A.9: Schema of the artiﬁcial lungs
==PAGE:126==
108
==PAGE:127==
APPENDIXB
Experimental conditions
B.1
Exhaled CO2
The typical breathing cycle consists of 2,5 s inhalation, 2,5 s exhalation and 1 s break. During a whole session,
0,771 L/ min of CO2 was dosed to the exhaled air, which corresponds to CO2 pollution load per occupant
according to CEN CR 1752 [2]. This resulted in CO2 concentration of 53 570 ppm in the exhaled air. The
Appendix B.1 presents the determination of the dosed amount of CO2.
The background parameters considered for the calculations were CO2 gas constant R of 188,91 J/(kgK),
total pressure (including atmospheric pressure and gauge pressure before the rotameter) p of 101 015 Pa and
temperature of the gas right before the rotameter TCO2 of 296,55 K (i.e. 23,4 °C).
The input generation rate was 9,66 mg/s, which leads to 23,18 mg/s during 2,5 s exhalation period of one
breathing cycle.
GCO2 = 9,66mg/s = 579,5mg/min = 57,95mg/cycle = 57,95/2,5s =
= 23,18mg/sduring the exhalation period of the breathing cycle
(B.1)
Based on the conditions mentioned above, this CO2 generation results in the gas ﬂow qCO2 of 0,771 L/min.
qCO2 = GCO2 × R × T
p
(B.2)
qCO2 = 23,18mg/s × 188,91J/(kgK) × 296,55K
101015Pa
= 12,86mL/s =
= 0,771L/min
(B.3)
The dosing of CO2 to the exhaled air of the manikin was constant in time. The solenoid valve in the system
ensured that the gas was dosed in the clean air in the exhalation circuit only during the exhalation period of
2,5 s. During the rest 3,5 s, the CO2 was released out of the system. The operation of the artiﬁcial lungs is
described in detail in Appendix A.
To determine the CO2 concentration in exhaled air of the manikin in ppm, the actual air ﬂow of the exhaled
air must be included. The exhalation pump was working with constant ﬂow rate qexh of 14,4 L/min, i.e. 0,24 L/s.
A typical breathing ﬂow rate of a sedentary person is 6 L/min [57], which means 0,6 L/cycle. Considering 2,5 s
exhalation per cycle, the ﬁnal exhalation ﬂow is 0,24 L/s per exhalation period.
Determination of CO2 concentration in actual exhaled air is following

= 0,771L/min
0,24L/s × 60s × 1000000 = 535577ppm
(B.4)

B.2 Heat balances
111
B.2
Heat balances
The experiment was performed in 10 sessions with diﬀerent conditions, designed to investigate various im-
pacts on the CO2 exposure and the concentration distribution. Summary of the conditions and various factors
was described in 4.3. This appendix describes in more details single conditions, the heat balances, and the
designed power of the used systems.
For some conditions with mixing ventilation, solar gains were taken into account. Assumptions like build-
ing location, orientation, occupancy hours, type of the window and shadings, etc. were done to determine
the solar gains through windows to be 561 W. In the chamber, 38 % of solar loads was simulated as direct
solar loads, provided by electric foils placed underneath the PVC ﬂooring. Remaining 62 % were provided
by the artiﬁcial window made from the radiant panels. The desired surface temperature was calculated from
Equation 4.1 and monitored by the temperature sensors PT100. Two sensors were placed on each panel, i.e.
10 sensors in total, on their colder and warmer part according to the hot water supply direction. The surface
temperature for the calculation was then assumed to be an average of all 10 sensors.
Part of the heat load was covered by cooler supply air, provided at 18 °C, 18,5 °C and 22 °C for various ven-
tilation systems. The rest of the cooling demand was covered by the chilled ceiling panels. The desired surface
temperature of the chilled ceiling was determined by using Equation 4.2 and the known cooling demand.
During performing the experiment it was observed, that the power provided by the occupants diﬀered
from day to day. In order to maintain the desired room air temperature of 26 °C, power of the chilled ceiling
was slightly adjusted in the beginning of each session if needed. The set-points for the artiﬁcial window and
the supply air stayed unchanged.

APPENDIXC
Calibration
All the measuring equipment was calibrated prior to the experiment. The calibration of the measuring in-
struments used in the experiment was performed in three diﬀerent chambers in ICIEE, namely chamber 1, 2,
and 3. Chamber 1 had dimensions of 3,6 m x 2,5 m x 2,5 m and was built out of stainless steel. The air was
supplied by a perforated ﬂoor area. This chamber was used for calibration of instruments measuring CO2
concentration. Chamber 2 was identical to chamber 1 and was used for calibration of anemometers in a wind
tunnel. Chamber 3 had dimensions of 6,0 m x 4,7 m x 2,5 m. Just as in case of chambers 1 and 2, the clean air
was supplied through the ﬂoor area, i.e. piston ﬂow. The chamber was constructed to ensure a mean radiant
temperature equal to the room air temperature and to negligible radiant temperature asymmetry. Appendix C
presents the calibration processes for all used measuring equipment.
C.1
CO2 concentration
C.1.1
Multipoint Sampler and Doser INNOVA
The calibration of INNOVAs was performed in chamber 1, at 25 °C, by using N2O and CO2 gases. The reference
gas was supplied into a 1 L jar, with a small fan inside. The fan ensured full mixing in the jar. The INNOVAs
were placed outside of the chamber. The air sampling tubes led into the chamber and into the sealed jar. In
the beginning, N2O was dosed into the jar, in order to empty it from any CO2. This was the ﬁrst point of
calibration with CO2 concentration equal to 0 ppm. Consequently, the reference gas was dosed in to reach con-
centration levels of 500 ppm, 1 000 ppm, and 1 500 ppm, respectively. The concentration was measured by the
INNOVA sets for at least 30 min after reaching the steady state. Although the software presents the measured
values in ppm, the data were exported in mg/m3. To obtain the correction for the calibration, the measured
concentrations in mg/m3 were plotted against the reference gas concentration in ppm for each INNOVA. The
linear relationship was described by a trend line, which was later used for converting all measured data to
ppm. The linear relations are Equations C.1, C.2, and C.3 for INNOVA 2, 4, and 5, respectively.
y = 0,5225x + 10,356
(C.1)
y = 0,543x −7,3384
(C.2)
y = 0,507x + 6,9024
(C.3)
Later, after each session, the data measured by INNOVAs were exported and collected. The exported data
were corrected by applying above mentioned equations for each INNOVA. This way, the exported data were
transferred from mg/m3 into ppm and calibrated at the same time. Figure C.1 presents the calibration curve
for INNOVA 2 as an example.
C.1.2
Vaisala Transmitters
Vaisalas transmitters were inter-calibrated with INNOVAs prior to the experiment in chamber 1. The nine
transmitters were placed in a tight sealed aquarium together with a small fan in it. The calibration procedure
was the same as in case for INNOVAs. A linear trend line was deﬁned for each transmitter and all measured
==PAGE:136==

C.2
Temperature and relative humidity
Two types of temperature sensors were used in the experiment - RTDs PT100 and Sensirion SHT31-DIS. The
sensors were attached to a surface to measure the surface temperature or placed in the chamber to measure
the air temperature. The two diﬀerent purposes deﬁned the calibration procedure for the sensors.
The calibration of sensors for surface temperature was carried out in chamber 5 and the procedure was fol-
lowing. The RTD sensors were collected and placed into a small insulated box, without touching any surface.
The box was enclosed and the CTH6500 probe was inserted inside as well, to measure the reference temper-
ature. The temperature in the chamber was step-by-step adjusted to 6 diﬀerent temperatures - 21 °C, 23 °C,
25 °C, 27 °C, 29 °C and 31 °C. The mean temperature was deﬁned as the average of 10 minutes measurement at
each reference temperature for every single sensor. A calibration curve for each sensor separately was found
by plotting the measured temperatures against the reference temperature from CTH6500.
The sensors for measuring air temperature were calibrated in chamber 3. The sensors were placed in a
short metal duct, protecting the sensors from radiant eﬀect from surrounding electric equipment, as shown in
Figure C.2. Constant air movement around the sensors was ensured by several fans placed around the duct.
==PAGE:137==
C.2 Temperature and relative humidity
119
Figure C.2: RTD sensors prepared for the calibration in chamber 3
The thermometer CTH6500 was monitoring the reference temperature with its probe located in the middle of
the duct together with the PT100 sensors. The calibration was performed under 5 reference temperatures -
22 °C, 23 °C, 26 °C, 28 °C and 30 °C. After the calibration was done, the same method for deﬁning the calibra-
tion curve as for surface temperature sensors was applied. Example of the calibration curve for PT100 sensor
is presented in Figure C.3.
Figure C.3: Calibration curve for PT100 sensor (ID-304) and the linear trend line
==PAGE:138==
120
C Calibration
C.3
Air speed
The transducers were calibrated using a small wind tunnel in the laboratory facilities of ICIEE, in chamber 2.
The wind tunnel, shown in Figure C.4, works together with a suitable software and a micromanometer mea-
suring the pressure diﬀerence. A transducer was attached the way so that the sensor was in the wind tunnel
with a free space around it. The transducer was then connected to a computer operating the wind tunnel.
After specifying the desired properties, the software ran the wind tunnel automatically, gradually increasing
the speed in the wind tunnel and monitoring the measured air speed. Consequentially, the software created
a calibration ﬁle and adjusted the probe itself. After the calibration, the data could be read directly from the
transducers operating software without any further corrections.
Figure C.4: Wind tunnel
C.4
Thermal manikin
The thermal manikin was calibrated for the absolute temperature and for the equivalent temperature prior to
the experiment. Both calibrations were performed in chamber 3.
Absolute temperature calibration was made automatically by the manikin’s software, by measuring under
two diﬀerent ambient temperatures. For this calibration, the manikin was completely naked, placed in the
chamber and seated on a supportive structure, with its legs and arms supported in the air. The set up is pre-
sented in Figure C.5. This way ensured that air can ﬂow freely around the whole body. There were three fans
surrounding the manikin, including one located under its legs. The fans secured a uniform air ﬂow around
the manikin. The manikin was operated in No heat mode. The room air temperature in the chamber was ﬁrst
adjusted to a lower desired temperature, i.e. 20 °C, which was monitored by thermometer CTH6500. The data
were logged and stated in the software as Calibration Temperature A, stating the exact temperature displayed
on the thermometer. Afterwards, the room air temperature was increased to 30 °C, and after reaching steady
state, logged in the software as Calibration Temperature B. The new temperature constants were calculated
automatically.
The equivalent temperature calibration of the manikin was performed under the expected conditions dur-
ing the experiment, i.e. the manikin was dressed with the same clothes as during the experiment, it was
seated on the same chair at the same table, with the same distance from the table. The set up is presented in
Figure C.6. That means that two calibrations were done in total - with distance of 0 cm and 10 cm from the
table. The equivalent temperature calibration required logging data at ﬁve diﬀerent ambient temperatures.
The actual room air temperature in the chamber was monitored by thermometer CTH6500. Both the surface
temperature and the power for each segment were used for calculation of hcal,i using Equation 4.10.
==PAGE:139==
C.4 Thermal manikin
